# Complete Guide to AWS Security and Compliance Management

> Understanding—and successfully implementing—security concepts is essential to using AWS as your enterprise solution. In this course, instructor Sharif Nijim couples pragmatic advice with practical examples that educate IT pros on how to create a secure infrastructure within Amazon Web Services. Explore the shared responsibility model of security, which splits duties between your company and AWS. D

> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management) | 11h | Intermediate | 2K learners

>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/aws-security-overview?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Sharif] Security and compliance are top concerns when preparing to use any cloud service. It's a thrilling time to be an IT professional. We continue to witness one of the most significant transformations in the history of IT as the global consolidation of data centers continues. Startups are born in the cloud while established companies continue to migrate away from their own data centers, leveraging the public cloud for their IT infrastructure needs. This course is designed to give you a comprehensive understanding of the security and compliance tools available within AWS and how to use them. Hi, I'm Sharif Nijim, and I'd like to welcome you to our complete guide to security and compliance in [[Amazon Web Services (AWS)|Amazon Web Services]]. Let's get started.

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>

You can build a system to support any business requirement by selecting from the available network, compute, database, and storage components. Since AWS is the engine that powers an increasing number of businesses, understanding how to keep your AWS ecosystem secure is imperative. Ensuring compliance is also essential in today's increasingly regulated environment. A general understanding of information technology concepts would help you visualize how to use AWS in a secure and compliant manner. While no prior AWS knowledge is required for this course, it certainly doesn't hurt. An understanding of server virtualization is useful, as is an understanding of access controls and identity management. Think of this course as selecting appetizer-sized morsels from an overflowing [[AWS Security]] and compliance buffet. You'll learn about security and compliance foundations, how AWS implements IAM, multi account considerations, how to detect and respond to security incidents, and the tools available to help protect your networks, systems, applications, and data. The themes of this course will resonate with existing enterprises.

Please bring your most curious mind to derive the greatest benefit from this course. Think deeply about your existing environment and how the concepts and tools we cover can improve your overall security and compliance posture. All right, let's go.

### 1. AWS Security Foundations

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Understanding shared responsibility](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-shared-responsibility?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-shared-responsibility?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's examine foundational security concepts for operating in [[Amazon Web Services (AWS)|Amazon Web Services]]. Before we get hands-on in the AWS console, it's important to understand the AWS shared responsibility model. You want to be confident you understand what AWS is responsible for and where that responsibility ends. AWS provides infrastructure services on a global scale. AWS subdivides the world into Regions. A Region is completely independent physical location, such as ap-south-1, located in Mumbai, India, and us-east-1, located in the US state of Virginia. Each Region contains at least two Availability Zones, or AZs. Each AZ is made up of at least one completely independent data center connected through redundant, low-latency, high-speed network links. Independent of Region or Availability Zone, AWS also has Edge Locations throughout the world, located in places like Osaka, Japan, Milano, Italy, Sao Paulo, Brazil, and South Bend, Indiana. These Edge Locations power CloudFront, the AWS Content Delivery Network. To address application use cases requiring low latency, AWS also offers Local Zones.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-shared-responsibility?u=76281980&t=97)** A Local Zone makes a subset of AWS services available where people are highly concentrated. For example, us-east-1-chi-1a is physically located near Chicago, Illinois. The combination of Regions, Availability Zones, and Edge Locations represent the AWS global infrastructure. AWS is completely responsible for the physical security controls of these layers. Riding on top of these layers are the services AWS makes available to its customers. From a broad categorical perspective, these offerings include various compute, database, networking, and storage services. For example, there are a number of compute services provided by AWS. These services include the Elastic Compute Cloud, or [[Amazon EC2|EC2]]. That's where you make virtual servers. The EC2 Container Service supports containerized workloads. Lambda is a serverless offering for purely event-driven programming. Elastic Beanstalk is a low-friction way to run and manage web applications. AWS has a number of storage offerings. The oldest and best-known is the Simple Storage Service, known as S3. S3 is an object storage offering,
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-shared-responsibility?u=76281980&t=191)** which I like to think of as the hard drive of the internet. EC2 Block Storage, or EBS, is used to provide local, block storage to EC2 servers inside AWS. Elastic File System, or EFS, is a paper-use file system storage offering, while Glacier is a cost-effective service intended for archival storage. AWS has many database offerings, including Relational Database Services, or RDS, which provides managed [[Relational Databases]], including [[MySQL]], [[Microsoft SQL Server|SQL Server]], and Oracle. DynamoDB is a managed [[NoSQL]] database. ElastiCache is available for in-memory caching, Redshift for [[Data Warehousing]], and Neptune for your graph database needs. Among the networking services, you'll find Virtual Private Cloud, which lets you create independent, isolated virtual networks. Think of a VPC as your virtual data center in the cloud. Route 53 provides [[Domain Name System (DNS)|domain name system]] services, and CloudFront provides a global content delivery network. AWS is responsible for the security of the cloud. That means the responsibility of securing all the global infrastructure and core services I just described lies on the shoulders of AWS. This is liberating for you since physical security controls and audits can be added
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-shared-responsibility?u=76281980&t=286)** to your list of things not to worry about. That said, what you decide to put in the cloud is another matter entirely. Most enterprises provide services where application and platform level controls are implemented using identity and access management. Services you operate rely on appropriate network, firewall, and operating system configurations. The data within these systems can be of varied [[Data Classification]] status, some of which require encryption. And of course, all enterprises possess valuable data. The security of how you configure as well as what you place and operate within AWS is your responsibility. Let's explore an example. Suppose one of your services is a typical three-tiered application. Looking at the AWS tools available, you decide to use Route 53 for DNS, CloudFront as your content delivery network, and S3 for storing static resources. You also decide to go with a load-balanced web tier, a load-balanced application tier, and RDS for your database needs. The security of Route 53, CloudFront, S3, the load balancers, EC2, and RDS services is the responsibility of AWS. Securing the data
>
> **[6:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-shared-responsibility?u=76281980&t=381)** and appropriately configuring each tool is on you. More specifically, you might be wondering what's on your plate. Your responsibility lies in the configuration and patching of the operating systems and software packages you put on EC2 servers. You're also responsible for security controls for each of the compute, database, storage, and networking components you use. For example, if you use the S3 storage offering, you're responsible for its access controls. You're also responsible for configuring identity and access management. This is true for administrative access for your CIS ops personnel, as well as application access for your end users. Looking ahead, you can focus your attention on securing what you put into the cloud, while you can [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|rest]] easy knowing that Amazon Web Services is maintaining the security of the components you use.

>

#### [Understanding the AWS security landscape](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-the-aws-security-landscape?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-the-aws-security-landscape?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's take a quick look at the pallet of security-related tools that are available within AWS. AWS groups its offerings into four main categories of security tools. Identity and access management, or IAM is in my mind, the most foundational security tool AWS provides. It allows you to configure users, groups, and permissions. It also lets you configure single sign-on and share resources you use in AWS. AWS offers a number of detective controls to help you understand what's going on in your ecosystem. This includes everything from [[Data Classification]] and [[Application Security]] to threat detection and vulnerability scanning. In terms of infrastructure protection, AWS has offerings that offer protection from distributed denial of service attacks, as well as more granular controls for filtering web traffic. In terms of data protection, AWS provides tools for the creation and management of encryption keys, as well as SSL/TLS certificate management. AWS also provides a variety of [[Network Security]] controls. When you create VPCs within AWS, you have the ability to apply network access control lists, or NACLs. Acting as a firewall, NACLs allow you to control
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-the-aws-security-landscape?u=76281980&t=93)** inbound and outbound network traffic in a stateless manner. Security groups are controls that apply to services like [[Amazon EC2|EC2]] instances, application load balancers, and managed [[Relational Databases]]. Security groups act as a stateful virtual firewall, which you can configure to allow network traffic on ports you specify.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Data Classification]] (1), [[Application Security]] (1), [[Network Security]] (1), [[Amazon EC2|Ec2]] (1), [[Relational Databases]] (1)
> **Env Vars:** aws (9), iam (1), ssl (1), tls (1), ec2 (1)
> **CLI Commands:** aws (9)
> **Prerequisites:** configure (3)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### [Understanding separation of duties](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-separation-of-duties?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-separation-of-duties?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Now that we have an understanding of the AWS shared responsibility model and an awareness of the tools for configuring security controls, let's explore how to configure an AWS account in accordance with best practices. What better place to start than by implementing separation of duties and multifactor authentication. Separation of duties is a concept that requires more than one person to perform an action. Implemented properly, separation of duties significantly reduces the chances of a security compromise. Auditors love separation of duties, and for a good reason. Implementing this best practice within your AWS account will definitely get you style points when it comes time for your annual [[IT Audit]]. Let's explore how to protect your account by implementing separation of duties in concert with multifactor authentication. When you sign up for an AWS account and log in for the first time, you enter your email address and password. These two items combine to make up your root account credentials. As the name implies, these root credentials are all powerful. With root access, you can perform any action within your account up to and including deleting it. The combination of an email address
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-separation-of-duties?u=76281980&t=93)** and a password simply do not offer enough protection. You wouldn't have just an email and password standing between you and the destruction of all of your IT assets, would you? Besides, it is an accepted security best practice to implement multifactor authentication for all privileged accounts. You probably already have MFA configured for systems you currently have access to. On the personal side, it's a good idea to implement MFA for your [[LinkedIn]] account, [[Google]] account, Apple ID, and any financial organization. Let's outline how to dive in and get that root account protected. The very first thing you want to do is procure a physical multifactor authentication device. Why a physical device? Because it's something you are going to put in a safe place and keep track of every time it's used. While you are awaiting the arrival of the MFA device, identify the two groups in your organization which will be responsible for implementing separation of duties for root access. Task the first team, say Engineering, with knowing, maintaining, and rotating the password for the root account. Task the second team, say Information Security, with maintaining, logging, and controlling access to the physical MFA device. After MFA device arrives, it's time to enable MFA on your root account. Let's visualize the end-state authentication workflow.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-separation-of-duties?u=76281980&t=192)** In this case, the Engineering team has the password. Meanwhile, the Information Security team has the MFA device. Since the Engineering team can't access the MFA device without Information Security, and since Information Security doesn't have the root password, it's not possible for each team to gain independent access to the AWS account. Only by having these teams work together, with Engineering providing the password, and Information Security providing the MFA token, is access to the root account possible.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

> **CLI Commands:** aws (5), make (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1), is an  (1)
> **Best Practices:** best practice (2)
> **Prerequisites:** configure (1)

#### [Understanding CloudTrail](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-cloudtrail?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-cloudtrail?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] CloudTrail is a service within AWS that provides comprehensive API logging capabilities. Let's explore what CloudTrail is, what it can do for you, and how to get it configured. CloudTrail keeps track of changes in an AWS account. This is an indispensable service when considering compliance obligations. It is enabled by default when a new AWS account is created. Included for free are 90 days worth of activity. This activity can be examined in the AWS web console. Ultimately, CloudTrail activity resides in an S3 bucket. If you have multiple accounts, you can configure it such that all trails are delivered to the same bucket. CloudTrail has different categories in terms of the types of events that it logs. One type is management events, which record changes made along the AWS control plane. Example management events include setting up MFA, creating IAM users and creating [[Amazon EC2|EC2]] instances. Data events record information along the AWS data plane. In plain English, data events capture low level details about resource activity. That said, there is an additional charge. For example, a data event for S3 can capture [[Metadata]] about every time an S3 object
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-cloudtrail?u=76281980&t=95)** is accessed or a Lambda function fires. As you can imagine, data events can accumulate very quickly. Insights events is where machine learning meets audit logs. For an extra charge, CloudTrail Insights examines CloudTrail events and can detect anomalous activity based on deviation from what is normal for your account. Let's walk through an example. AWS famously uses [[Application Programming Interfaces (API)|application programming interfaces]] or APIs as the foundation for internal communications. APIs provide a consistent way to interact with AWS services from a variety of different sources. For instance, suppose you want to interact programmatically with AWS Elastic Compute Cloud or EC2 instances. EC2 instances are virtual servers running in AWS. AWS provides [[Software Development]] kits or SDKs for a variety of popular programming languages, including [[Java]], [microsoft.net](https://microsoft.net), Ruby, [[Python (Programming Language)|Python]], and [[PHP]] to name a few. Under the covers, all of these SDKs use the same backend API to interact with EC2. Even better, AWS's user-facing tools, including the command line interface and the web console, both use the same backend API to communicate with EC2. This is where CloudTrail comes in.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-cloudtrail?u=76281980&t=192)** Once configured, CloudTrail records API interactions and stores those interactions in an S3 bucket in your account. CloudTrail stores critical details about every event. These details include the time the event took place, the source IP address of the caller, the identity of the caller, and the resources referenced. As you can appreciate, CloudTrail represents a very powerful ally when the time comes to audit activity in your AWS account.

#### [Understanding CloudWatch](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-cloudwatch?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-cloudwatch?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] CloudWatch is the monitoring service built into AWS, capable of monitoring almost every AWS service. You can use it to engineer a comprehensive, proactive monitoring solution. Understanding what's happening in your environment is an essential security and audit best practice. Let's explore the components that make up this very useful service. Let's first explore CloudWatch metrics. Most AWS services integrate with CloudWatch by default. Since [[Amazon EC2|EC2]] is one of the most widely used AWS services, let's look at it first. Included in the cost of running an EC2 instance is basic CloudWatch monitoring. Without the need to install any software agents on the guest operating system, basic monitoring is collected at five-minute intervals. Basic monitoring gives you visibility into a limited set of metrics, including CPU utilization, disc activity and network activity. Also included for free are status check metrics. Status check metrics indicate the relationship between the EC2 instance and the underlying hypervisor. If the hypervisor deems an EC2 instance to be unhealthy, the status check metrics would show it. If a five-minute interval doesn't provide the granularity
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-cloudwatch?u=76281980&t=95)** you need, it is possible to enable detailed monitoring. While detailed monitoring can collect additional data points, it comes at an added cost. Detailed monitoring collects metrics at one-minute intervals. With detailed monitoring, you get the same CPU, disc, network and status check metrics that you get with basic monitoring. In order to get this increased level of granularity, there is a charge for enabling detailed monitoring. You may operate hundreds of servers, but really only need the increased granularity on a handful of them. Not to worry, as detailed monitoring can be enabled on a per instance basis. You may be wondering why memory isn't included in either basic or detailed monitoring. Since AWS has no visibility into what's running on an EC2 instance, CloudWatch doesn't have a way to access memory information as reported by an operating system. In order to gather memory information, you need to implement a CloudWatch agent or a custom metric. Let's talk a bit about additional CloudWatch capabilities, starting with custom metrics. As with most things AWS, CloudWatch is exposed via an API. With the proper message formatting,
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-cloudwatch?u=76281980&t=187)** you can configure custom metrics to send to CloudWatch. AWS recognizes that memory utilization is an important metric to have visibility into when looking at overall system health. If you want to gather memory metrics, AWS provides a CloudWatch software agent, which you can install on either [[Linux]] or [[Windows]] operating systems. You can also deploy the CloudWatch software agent to servers that you may have on-premises. Application logs are another important record of system health. CloudWatch can ingest log files. This lets you raise alerts based on error messages in your logs. Gathering metrics is not very useful unless you have the ability to trigger an alarm based on changing values. You can configure two types of alarms in CloudWatch. A passive alarm may simply pass along a notification that an event happened. For instance, you could trigger a notification if CPU utilization on an instance exceeds 50%. More interestingly, you can configure CloudWatch to take action based on an alarm state. For example, if the hypervisor health check goes into an error state, you can configure CloudWatch to attempt an instance recovery.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-cloudwatch?u=76281980&t=281)** While automated alerting and proactive responses are very useful features, sometimes you just want to get an overall look at the health of your system. To that end, you can configure customized [[Dashboards]] in CloudWatch. With a dashboard, you can select the metrics you want to see. Even better, you can overlay multiple EC2 instances on the same dashboard. This is particularly useful for identifying outliers across a fleet of machines performing the same function. The last concept I'd like to emphasize is the ability to take action based off of CloudWatch events. When a CloudWatch event fires, you can configure it to trigger a custom lambda function. By combining the power of these two services, you can build a truly event-driven monitoring system that enhances your overall security posture.

#### [Understanding CloudWatch alarms](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-cloudwatch-alarms?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-cloudwatch-alarms?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Before jumping in and using CloudWatch, it's important to understand CloudWatch Alarms in a bit more detail. As always, understanding how something works lets you use it in an optimal way. There are three states that a CloudWatch Alarm can be in. The first state is OK. For instance, suppose you create an alarm that triggers when CPU utilization on an [[Amazon EC2|EC2]] instance exceeds 75%. Anytime the CPU utilization is under 75%, the alarm would be in an OK state. The second state is ALARM. Extending our example, if the CPU utilization on an EC2 instance exceeds 75%, the CloudWatch Alarm's state would change to ALARM. The third state is INSUFFICIENT_DATA. Suppose an EC2 instance monitored by CloudWatch is only used to process a batch workload during a specific window. Making efficient use of your AWS and financial resources, that EC2 instance is only turned on during the batch window. While the EC2 instance is off, the CloudWatch Alarm is in a state of INSUFFICIENT_DATA, since the instance isn't using any CPU. While understanding the functionality is important, it only makes sense to create a CloudWatch Alarm
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-cloudwatch-alarms?u=76281980&t=94)** if you want someone or something to be notified When there is a State Change. Transitioning to any of the three possible alarm states can trigger an alert. Most commonly, people want to be alerted when a CloudWatch Alarm enters the ALARM state. CloudWatch Alarms are typically created to enter the ALARM state when expected thresholds are exceeded. INSUFFICIENT_DATA is another state you may want to be made aware of. For example, suppose you have an EC2 instance that is supposed to be on all the time. An errant DevOp accidentally shuts the instance down. This causes the CloudWatch Alarm on that instance to enter the INSUFFICIENT_DATA state. Since that is unexpected, you might want to receive an alert to that effect. It's possible to trigger an event when the CloudWatch Alarm state changes to OK. Although it's technically possible to do this, monitoring is commonly done on a managed-by-exception basis. Once the ALARM state is triggered, a notification is sent out. Most notifications are handled via Simple Notification Service or SNS messages. Recall that there are many ways to subscribe to an SNS topic, so there is a lot of flexibility here in terms of how you configure a reaction to an alarm. Another notification target is an Auto Scaling policy. For example, you may want to scale in or scale out
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-cloudwatch-alarms?u=76281980&t=191)** based on a state condition you define in CloudWatch. The next thing to understand is notification frequency. With SNS, a single message is sent when the alarm state changes. With Auto Scaling, the action is triggered as long as the alarm is in its new state. One thing to keep in mind when creating alarms is the consecutive period feature. Appropriate use of this feature will help you find the right balance between receiving alerts and too much noise. Suppose you want to be alerted when CPU utilization on an EC2 instance exceeds 75%. If CPU utilization spikes above 75% on occasion, that is likely a part of normal variable load on the server. However, if CPU spikes above 75% and stays there for an extended period, then it's something to be investigated.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Amazon EC2|Ec2]] (7)
> **Env Vars:** cpu (7), ec2 (7), alarm (5), insufficient_data (4), sns (3)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2), is an  (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (2), for instance (1)
> **CLI Commands:** aws (1), find (1)
> **Warnings:** keep in mind (1)
> **Prerequisites:** configure (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### [Configuring Simple Notification Service](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/configuring-simple-notification-service?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/configuring-simple-notification-service?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Presenter] It's generally a good idea to use a notification service as notifications are often helpful when identifying the root cause of irregular activities. For issuing alerts, CloudWatch makes use of simple notification service, or SNS. SNS is a push messaging service that is part of the AWS ecosystem. Let's talk a bit about how SNS works as I'll use it for issuing CloudWatch alerts. As the name implies, SNS is quite simple to use. To get started, you just need to create a topic. When CloudWatch issues alerts with SNS, it publishes them to a topic. If you want to receive that message, you can simply subscribe to that topic. Topic subscriptions can take many [[Forms]] including an HTTP endpoint and email address. Push notifications to a mobile app and SMS messages. Within the AWS ecosystem. Simple queue service queues and lambda functions can also subscribe to an SNS topic. Once a topic has been created and is subscribed to, all you have to do is publish a message to that topic. Now let's get into the AWS console and walk through setting up a new topic as well as a subscription to that topic. Here I am at my web console homepage.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/configuring-simple-notification-service?u=76281980&t=96)** The service I want to use is Simple Notification Service, so I simply type SNS in the search bar. Selecting the simple notification service link takes me to the homepage for SNS. The first thing I want to do is show any existing topics that I may have. Expanding the left-hand navigation. I select the topics link. Here I can see, I don't have any existing topics in this account. To get started, I go ahead and click the create topic button. This opens up the create topic page. Notice that the default option is for a First in, first out simple queue service queue. In this case, I'm interested in creating a topic that I will subscribe to via email. Therefore, I select the standard topic type. Note that FIFO is only available for SQS Queues, while Standard has many more subscription protocols available to it. The first thing I need to do is specify the name of my topic. In this case, I'm going to specify security alerts. Note that optionally, I can configure display name for this topic. Since security alerts is relatively concise, I'm going to skip that for now. Expanding the encryption section, I can see
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/configuring-simple-notification-service?u=76281980&t=190)** that it's possible to configure SNS messages to be encrypted at [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|rest]], even though they're encrypted in flight by default. Since that's a good idea for security alerts, I'm going to enable it now by clicking the encryption toggle. Note that here, I can specify a custom key management service key. In this case, I'm just going to go with the default. You can see I also have options to configure who can access this topic, as well as a data protection policy. Expanding the HTTPS section, you could see that I can specify the number of retries as well as the minimum and maximum delay for retrying. >
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/configuring-simple-notification-service?u=76281980&t=287)** Note the warning message below the endpoint text box indicating that you have to confirm a subscription before you can receive messages. This makes sense as you wouldn't want to be able to spam someone's email that you don't have control over. Everything here looks good, so I go ahead and click the create subscription button. This takes me to the subscription summary page where I can see the details of my new subscription. Note the status as pending confirmation. Let's go ahead and fix that now by popping open my email. Here, you can see, I have a new subscription confirmation email from AWS Notifications. Opening that message, I can see that I've been subscribed to the security alerts topic. The only thing I need to do to confirm this subscription is click the confirm subscription link. Clicking that link opens up a new browser tab confirming that my subscription has been registered. Switching back to the AWS console, I simply refresh the subscription page. Note that the status has changed from pending confirmation to confirmed. That means, this subscription is now active and any message published to the topic will be delivered. Now, let's go ahead and validate
>
> **[6:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/configuring-simple-notification-service?u=76281980&t=381)** that everything is working as intended. To do so, I click the topics link in the left-hand navigation. Then I select the security alerts topic that I had just created. #### [Using a CloudWatch alarm](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/using-a-cloudwatch-alarm?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/using-a-cloudwatch-alarm?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] With an understanding that CloudWatch can raise an alert based off of an event, let's explore a practical use-case where CloudWatch comes into play. Suppose you have a website running on an [[Amazon EC2|EC2]] server. You've sized the web server to accommodate your normal expected traffic. To monitor CPU activity, we'll configure a CloudWatch alarm that will trigger when CPU utilization exceeds 75%. One day, a piece of content on your website goes viral, and a hoard of new users show up to access that content. You can let administrators know that the server is in duress using a CloudWatch alarm. When the CloudWatch alarm enters the alarm state, it will post a message to an SNS topic, which in turn will send a high-CPU-alert email. Let's dive into the console and make this happen. From the main AWS console, I navigate to CloudWatch. I type "CloudWatch" into the search bar. Clicking on the link for the service brings me to the homepage for CloudWatch. I don't currently have any CloudWatch alarms configured, so I go ahead and get started by clicking the "Create alarms" link. This brings me to the CloudWatch "Alarms" page. ### 2. IAM Concepts in AWS

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Understanding Identity and Access Management](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-identity-and-access-management?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-identity-and-access-management?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Understanding identity and access management is a crucial concept to the secure administration of an AWS account. Going forward, I'll refer to identity and access management simply by the acronym IAM. What is IAM and what is it used for? Generally speaking, IAM has two primary functions. The first function of IAM is to authenticate users. By validating the combination of a username and password, the authentication function of IAM is complete. For example, let's say you have a Netflix subscription for streaming movies and television shows. In order to log into your account, you have to supply your username and password. In the context of IAM and AWS, authentication is the combination of an IAM user and the user's security credential. The second function of IAM is the authorization of users. For example, suppose you have a valid username and password for Netflix, however, you are unable to stream any content because you have a single stream plan and one of your family members is actively streaming. That is, you are not authorized to access content despite the fact that you have authenticated successfully. In terms of IAM and AWS, authorization specifies the services that an IAM user has access to and the actions the IAM user can perform on those services.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-identity-and-access-management?u=76281980&t=97)** In AWS, the creation of IAM users allows you to give more than one person individualized, auditable access to services within your account. Suppose you have 100 people in your organization who need access to your account. Instead of the catastrophically bad idea of sharing the root account credentials with the group, each person would be configured with an individual IAM user. Attempting to manage authorization for hundreds of people independently is a nightmare for any system administrator. Fortunately, IAM and AWS supports the concept of groups. Remember the hundred people who need access to your account? Chances are, they already fall into existing organizational groups. These groups could include engineering, [[Quality Assurance]], information security, or finance. You'll want to consider existing group structure and how to project that into AWS IAM. Once you have your users mapped into groups, you can spend time considering the types of resources you'll be using in AWS and the permissions associated with these resources. For example, for [[Amazon EC2|EC2]], different permissions exist which grant the ability to stop, start, reboot, or terminate an instance. Once you've considered an aggregated permissions into logical collections, you can assign permissions to IAM policies.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-identity-and-access-management?u=76281980&t=191)** Remember, permissions are granular in nature and there are many possibilities for every service AWS offers. A policy is simply a collection of permissions you want to grant. Once you have the IAM policies in place, you can proceed to assign policies to IAM groups. As you establish IAM groups, you can assign individual IAM users to those groups. In AWS, policies allow you to manage access to resources in a very granular fashion. For instance, you might give your [[DevOps]] the ability to create, stop, and terminate EC2 instances. On the other hand, you likely want to prevent the finance department from taking any action in your account that is not billing-related. Keep job roles in mind as you design your IAM infrastructure to support it. Another important concept to internalize is that AWS IAM is built on the principle of least privilege. This means that, by default, access to a resource is denied. While AWS has many policy layers, let's step through a simplified identity-based policy evaluation process to see what happens when a user tries to access a resource. By default, an access request is presumed to be denied. Then, the IAM policies for the user resource combination in question are evaluated. If there is an explicit denial of that action in the policy, the user's access is denied,
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-identity-and-access-management?u=76281980&t=285)** and the evaluation workflow stops. An explicit deny trumps everything, period. Full stop. If there isn't an explicit deny in the policy, the evaluation process looks for an entry that explicitly allows the action in question. If there is an explicit allow, the action is permitted. Otherwise, access is denied. Remember, if you see an explicit deny statement in a policy, the associated actions are denied. Only if there is no explicit deny and an explicit allow is an action permitted. Since IAM is integrated across many AWS services, there are some additional nifty features that can be an asset when controlling access. For example, you have the ability to apply IAM policies to a role. Roles can be assigned to a specific resource. Say you create a policy that allows read-write access to an S3 bucket and assign that policy to a role. If you grant that role to an EC2 instance, it will have the ability to read and write to the specified S3 bucket without any embedded credentials. You can grant temporary access to resources using the secure token service. This is useful when you want someone to have access to resources on a time-limited basis. AWS IAM also supports identity federation, so if you already manage users external to AWS
>
> **[6:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-identity-and-access-management?u=76281980&t=381)** using something like [[Active Directory]], you can map active directory identities to IAM users.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Amazon EC2|Ec2]] (3), [[Active Directory]] (2), [[Quality Assurance]] (1), [[DevOps]] (1)
> **Env Vars:** iam (27), aws (14), ec2 (3)
> **CLI Commands:** aws (14)
> **Definitions:** is an  (3), is a  (2), means that (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (4), for instance (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### [Understanding IAM policies](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-iam-policies?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-iam-policies?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] IAM policies are a crucial tool for effective granular permissions management within AWS. IAM policies are used to control what happens when a given operation is executed against a resource within AWS. This control can be as granular as your organizational need. For example, suppose a company wants to give its newly hired [[DevOps]] engineers access to start, run, and stop [[Amazon EC2|EC2]] instances. However, in order to prevent accidental loss of work, the company wants to inhibit the ability to take any other action, especially instance termination. You can easily accommodate that use case with an IAM policy. Policies can be assigned to IAM users, groups or roles. This allows for the separation of the authoring of policies and their association with people or resources. To help with managing common authorization use cases, AWS provides a large number of managed policies. A good example is the read only access policy. Since it's maintained by AWS, as new services are rolled out, you can be assured that read only access to those services will be added to the policy without any action on your part. Policy documents are written in [[JavaScript]] Object Notation or [[JSON]], easy for humans to read and for machines to process.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-iam-policies?u=76281980&t=92)** It is necessary to become familiar with JSON in order to understand AWS managed policies or to create policies on your own. Finally, it's important to remember that authorization to a service is denied by default. If you have a complicated policy with both access and deny statements on the same resource, access to that resource will be denied. Bottom line, remember that an explicit deny always wins in IAM, There are many different types of IAM policies available to control access to resources. The most common type of policy is the identity-based policy. This policy type is attached to an IAM role, group or user. Say you want to grant a user access to a specific S3 bucket, you could attach a policy to the role, group, or the user itself to enable that access. Similar to identity-based policies or resource-based policies, let's say you want an EC2 instance to access an S3 bucket, you can assign a role to the instance itself, thereby avoiding any embedded credentials. Permission boundaries are a novel policy type that specify the maximum permissions granted by an identity-based policy. For example, suppose you've set the maximum permissions for a user to be limited to an S3 bucket. If the user somehow gets attached
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-iam-policies?u=76281980&t=186)** to a policy allowing access to EC2, the permission boundary policy will prevent that access from happening. You may find yourself using AWS Organizations, an incredibly useful tool for establishing operational consistency across multiple AWS accounts. Service control policies work similar to permission boundaries, except they apply at the account level. For example, say you have two child accounts within an organization. You could apply a service control policy, which would limit the maximum permissions across every account in the organization. Service control policies are great for managing permissions at scale. Access control lists are another tool for controlling access across accounts. There are also session-based policies that can be used to limit permissions for a specific session. Similar to permission boundaries and service control policies, session-based policies can't grant permissions, only limit them.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Amazon EC2|Ec2]] (3), [[JSON]] (2), [[DevOps]] (1), [[JavaScript]] (1)
> **Env Vars:** iam (7), aws (7), ec2 (3), json (2)
> **CLI Commands:** aws (7), find (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (3), similar to (2)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### [Configure IAM policies](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/configure-iam-policies?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/configure-iam-policies?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] With an understanding of the different types of IAM policies, let's get into the AWS console and create a custom identity based policy. The policy we're about to create will satisfy the use case of allowing a senior engineer the ability to start, stop and reboot any existing [[Amazon EC2|EC2]] instance in the account. As this is quite the powerful capability, it's not one to be assigned lightly. Starting from the main AWS console screen, I need to get over to Identity and Access Management or IAM. I type IAM in the search box and click the resulting link. This brings me to the IAM dashboard. From the IAM dashboard, I click the policies link in the left-hand navigation to get to the policies configuration screen. Here, I can see an abundance of existing policies. Looking at the filter by type, I can look at AWS managed by job function. AWS aggregates and maintains permissions needed for a variety of job roles. In the AWS managed section, you can see a variety of policies corresponding to different use cases within the AWS ecosystem. With over 1000 existing policies, I'm sure you can appreciate
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/configure-iam-policies?u=76281980&t=92)** how complicated policy authoring can be. To get started, I click the create policy button. When it comes to specifying permissions, note that I have a couple of options. I can either use the visual editor or edit directly in [[JSON]]. Clicking on the JSON tab would let me edit a policy directly using JSON. Clearly, I'd have to be very familiar with both JSON and the IAM policy document syntax. Since I'm not, I'm going to stick with the Visual Editor. Expanding the Choose a Service dropdown box, we can see that all existing AWS services are available. Scrolling down, you can see that there are a remarkable number of them. Fortunately, AWS lists the most commonly used services near the top. Since I'm working with EC2, I simply select EC2 to continue. First, let's click on the expand all link associated with policies and scroll down. It's immediately and abundantly clear that there is a remarkable degree of granularity that can be applied from a permissions standpoint. Fortunately, there is the ability to filter actions by simply typing them in. Scrolling back to the top, I click collapse all. Recalling that the default behavior is to deny actions,
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/configure-iam-policies?u=76281980&t=188)** I simply want this policy to have the ability to start, run, stop and reboot instances that already exist. To proceed, I type start in the filter actions search box and select start instances from the list. Now, let's do the same thing with stop, reboot and run.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/configure-iam-policies?u=76281980&t=219)** Scrolling down, we can see a couple of warnings. First, we see the message that run instances requires additional permissions. Clicking on the link, I can see that we need to resolve the ability to create tags and then pass an IAM role and get parameters from Systems Manager. Let's solve create tags first. Closing the modal window, I simply look for create tags within EC2. Before resolving PassRole and get parameters, let's take a look at the other messages within the resources section. Remember how AWS operates on the principle of least privilege? This policy generator is trying to lock down the ability to start, stop and reboot a specific instance identified by its Amazon resource name or ARN. Depending on your use case, you may want to follow these prompts to further restrict what this policy can do. For example, you may want to restrict action to within a VPC or subnet or to a specific instance. Since my intent is to be broadly permissive with this policy, I'm going to roll it up and change the radio button from specific to all. Since this affects any EC2 instance in the account, let's expand the request conditions section and make sure that anybody using this policy has MFA.
>
> **[5:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/configure-iam-policies?u=76281980&t=318)** I certainly wouldn't want the ability to manipulate EC2 instances in account behind only a simple password. Okay, now let's resolve the PassRole and get parameters dependencies. To do so, I click the add more permissions button. Let's resolve PassRole first. Dropping down the drop box, I select IAM and then type PassRole. Once again, I get the warning that it's wise to be more restrictive with this permission. However, for this demonstration, I'm going to specify all resources. Similarly, I'm going to augment this to ensure that multifactor authentication is present. Finally, let's resolve get parameters by adding more permissions. Notice that Systems Manager is not a commonly used service. Fortunately, I can simply type Systems Manager and select it from the list. Selecting get parameters should resolve all of my dependencies. Once again, I'm going to specify the all button and make sure that MFA is required. Scrolling down, I can see that there are no errors or warnings in my policy, however, there is a security notification. Clicking on it, I can see that it is warning me
>
> **[6:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/configure-iam-policies?u=76281980&t=414)** against the perils of using PassRole with an asterisk as that is broadly permissive. However, since that's my intent, I'm going to ignore that warning and proceed by clicking next. The next thing I need to do is specify a name for the policy. I'll go ahead and intuitively name this all-EC2-start-run-stop-reboot. In the description field, I'll describe what the policy does, namely that this policy is intended to be used by senior engineers, allowing the ability to run, start, stop and reboot any existing EC2 instance in this account. Okay, everything looks good, so I can go ahead, scroll to the bottom and click create policy. I get the confirmation message that my policy was created. If I want to see it, I can simply select customer managed from the filter by type dropdown. The customer managed section would show any and all policies that I authored myself. Clicking on the link for the policy brings me to the details screen for that policy. If I want to see the JSON that the visual policy generator created, I can do so by clicking the JSON link. Scrolling down, I can see each policy section,
>
> **[8:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/configure-iam-policies?u=76281980&t=508)** the permissions allowed and the fact that [[Multi-factor Authentication]] is required. IAM policies are tools that allow a remarkable degree of specificity for managing access to AWS resources. The interface I just walked through illustrates how complex policies can get. When constructing policies for the first time, remember to take your time to deeply understand what you want to do and the best way to efficiently accomplish your goals. Make sure you use conditions to further restrict when policies can be used. For example, you might want to specify that multifactor authentication must be in place. Be prepared to refactor your policies as you learn more, use new services or as new services are released. Make sure to experiment with policies in a non-production AWS account. Finally, remember that when writing policies, a deny action always wins. It is not uncommon to have multiple policies applied to a user or group. Suppose policy number one allows read and write to S3, while policy number two denies write access to S3. In that situation, the result will be that the user or group will not be able to write to S3 since a deny always wins. Remember, for any situation that requires access restriction
>
> **[10:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/configure-iam-policies?u=76281980&t=605)** to AWS resources, if you can imagine it, you can write a policy to handle it.

#### [Understanding IAM groups](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-iam-groups?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-iam-groups?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] An important step in your AWS journey is the mapping out of your IAM group structure. Let's dive right in by taking an example organization chart and mapping it into IAM groups. Taking a look at this sample org chart, we see five distinct groups, management, engineering, [[Quality Assurance]], information security, and finance. It is tempting to simply reflect the group structure as is into IAM. However, before creating IAM groups, it's worth thinking a bit more deeply about each individual box. For each group on the org chart, let's ponder a few questions. First off, do all people in a given group need the same permissions? That's unlikely, as any operational team typically has a leader. Do groups need to be split apart to accommodate different job roles? For example, are all engineering personnel the same? Do they all need equivalent access to all systems? Should new hires in the organization have the same access as experienced staff? What about different project teams? What about non-technical personnel? Do the same people who are responsible for paying the bills also analyze overall spend by service? What about individual group leaders? Do they need read only visibility into the cost of the resources they're using? For example, the engineering group might have 10 people in it,
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-iam-groups?u=76281980&t=93)** only two of whom require the ability to alter network configurations. Therefore, you may wind up with an engineering admin group that has IAM permissions to alter both [[Amazon EC2|EC2]] and VPC resources, with the regular engineering group's IAM permissions limited to being able to alter EC2.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>

> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/configure-iam-groups?u=76281980&t=188)** I also want the ability for folks in this group to change their passwords. If I had additional groups I needed to create, I could simply iterate through the web console. Of course, as is the case with most things in AWS, IAM Group management can be automated programmatically using the command line interface.

#### [Configuring a password policy](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/configuring-a-password-policy?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/configuring-a-password-policy?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Invariably, you'll have IAM users that need to interact with the browser-based AWS Management console. Prior to granting them access, you'll want to think through an appropriate password policy. There are a number of factors which are important when considering a password policy. The first is the complexity of the password itself. For example, you'll want to consider the password's overall length. You will also want to think through your character requirements in terms of uppercase, lowercase, and special characters. You will also need to think through the overall password lifecycle. For example, do you want to force passwords to expire, and if so, with what frequency? Who can change a password? And do you want to prevent the reuse of passwords? To set up a password policy, I've logged into the AWS console with my root credentials, and I'm starting at the IAM Dashboard. To implement a password policy, I click on the Account Settings link in the left hand navigation under Access Management. Here, we can see that this account is using the default password policy. We can also see some of the characteristics of that default password policy. Let's go ahead and implement a custom policy.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/configuring-a-password-policy?u=76281980&t=95)** To get started, I click the Edit button. Here, I have a couple of options. I see the IAM default password requirements, but I want to customize them, so I select the Custom radio button. A minimum password length of eight characters is sufficient for my purposes. I'm also going to require an uppercase letter, a lowercase letter, and at least one number. I'm also going to enable password expiration. In this case, I want passwords to expire every 60 days. I do want individual users to be able to change their own passwords, as well as prevent the reuse of passwords. While you can specify up to 24 previous passwords for AWS to remember, I'm going to specify six. Now that I've set up my password policy, I go ahead and click Save Changes. I do get a modal window that warns me that this policy will affect all existing users when they change their passwords, as well as any users upon creation. Since that's what I want to do, I go ahead and click Set Custom. This takes me back to the account settings page where I get the confirmation that the password requirements have been updated. I can also see the details of my own custom password policy. Now that I've updated the password policy,
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/configuring-a-password-policy?u=76281980&t=190)** I also want to customize the sign-in link so people within my account don't have to remember the account number. To do so, I navigate back to the IAM Dashboard. To customize the sign-in link, I need to create an account alias. To make one, I click the Create link near the right side of the screen. I type in my preferred alias, then click the Create Alias button. Immediately I get the confirmation message that my alias has been accepted. Note the sign-in URL for IAM users in this account. Instead of having an account number as part of the sign-in URL, it's been replaced by my account alias. That's something I can copy and distribute through email, [[Slack]], or whatever communication channel I have at hand.

#### [Configure IAM users: Web console](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/configure-iam-users-web-console?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/configure-iam-users-web-console?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In order for your organization to operate securely and effectively in AWS, it is important to configure each person as an independent IAM user. While it is possible to federate AWS with an external identity provider like [[Active Directory]], I am going to focus on a configuration where master user identity data is managed within AWS IAM. Most AWS services can be configured in a number of ways. For creating users, it's possible to automate with a script using the command line interface, or [[CLI]]. If you are a [[Windows]] system administrator and are more comfortable with [[Powershell]], AWS provides tools for Windows PowerShell. There is also an IAM web service that's possible to call over HTTP. It's also possible to simply create IAM users using the web console. While it's definitely best to federate or automate, I'm going to start off using the web console to demonstrate the user creation steps. A typical engineering group is composed of many people. In this example, Luca and Olivia are [[Linux]] specialists and will need the ability to operate [[Amazon EC2|EC2]] servers. Meanwhile, Madeline and Enzo, in addition to having Linux expertise, are also network administrators.

First, I want to validate my group structure, so I'll go ahead and click the User Groups link in the left-hand nav under Access Management. I'm going to ahead and let AWS generate a password for me. I'm also going to require Luca to change his password

Everything looks good here, so I go ahead and click the Next button to continue. From a permission standpoint, I have three options. One is to add users to a group. The other is to copy existing permissions from an individual user. The third option is to directly attach policies to an individual user. I want to stay away from direct attached policies. Instead, I'd like to manage permissions at the group level. To add Luca to the engineering group, I go ahead and click the checkbox next to the engineering group near the center of the screen. >
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/configure-iam-users-cli?u=76281980&t=93)** Looking at the script output, we can see that the user was successfully created. Note that the secret access key is displayed only on this screen. If it scrolls off the screen, it will be lost forever. In that case, I would have to deactivate the key, delete it, and generate a new one for her. So it's important for me to take that down for secure transmission. Back in the AWS console, let's confirm that she exists. I do so by clicking the user's link from the IAM dashboard. As expected, the Olivia user has been created. Pulling up the detail page for Olivia, I see that she has the same IAM permissions by virtue of her membership in the engineering group. While it is possible to create users manually using the web console, doing so quickly becomes tedious. In order to make the most efficient use of your time and to drive consistency, automate the creation of users using one of the three available methods. Recall that to configure MFA, each person will have to be physically present so they can scan the QR code. Finally, avoid generating access keys unless the user needs to interact with AWS in a programmatic fashion. While this may be required for engineers,
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/configure-iam-users-cli?u=76281980&t=189)** it likely isn't the case for folks in the finance department.

#### [IAM challenge](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/iam-challenge?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/iam-challenge?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] With an understanding of Identity and Access Management policies, groups and users, it's time for an IAM Challenge. The first step in this challenge is to create a new engineering-admin group. The intention of this group is to enable management of [[Amazon EC2|EC2]] as well as VPC resources. When creating this group, assign four AWS managed policies: EC2FullAccess, VPCFullAccess, ReadOnly, and IAMChangePassword. Once you have set up the group, create two new users, Madeline and Enzo. For both of these users, enable programmatic as well as web access. Finally, assign these.

#### [IAM challenge solution](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/iam-challenge-solution?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/iam-challenge-solution?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Now that you have taken on the challenge, let's take a peek at one way to solve it. Recall, we needed to create a new group, assign some managed policies, create users, enable programmatic and console access, and then assign those users to a group. I chose to tackle this challenge by creating a script using the Amazon Command Line Interface. I'm going to pop this open in a terminal and walk through the steps. First thing I do is create the engineering admin group, which I called engineering-admin. I then proceed to use the iam attach-group-policy command to attach the EC2FullAccess policy. This will permit full access over [[Amazon EC2|EC2]] resources. Since this is an Amazon managed policy, any underlying changes will persist when Amazon makes them. I then proceed to attach the VPCFullAccess, ReadOnlyAccess, and IAMUserChangePassword policies. With my group created and policies attached, I then use the create-user command to create both Madeline and Enzo. I proceed to use the add-user-to-group command to assign both Madeline and Enzo to the engineering-admin group.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/iam-challenge-solution?u=76281980&t=95)** I also specify an initial password for both Madeline and Enzo. >

>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/configuring-iam-roles?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] With a solid understanding of what IAM rules are, let's get into the web console and set up a new role to allow [[Amazon EC2|EC2]] instances to interact with S3. To get started, I click the Create Role button, which takes me through the Create Role Wizard. Here I can specify the type of entity I want to trust. For instance, I can trust a native AWS service, like EC2 or Lambda. I have the option of trusting another AWS account. This is useful when operating in a multi-account environment. You also have the option of trusting a web identity, using Open ID or AWS'S Cognito service. You also have the option of federating access using SAML, which stands for Security Assertion Markup Language. Finally, you can author a custom policy if you know how to write the [[JSON]] directly. #### [Extending CloudWatch](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/extending-cloudwatch?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/extending-cloudwatch?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] CloudWatch has the ability to collect metrics from a number of AWS services. However, to get a comprehensive understanding of the operational state of your [[Amazon EC2|EC2]] instances, the stock CloudWatch metrics may not be enough. Let's explore how you can take advantage of the CloudWatch agent to track any metric you want, so you can get the insights you need. Let's walk through an example. We know that CloudWatch doesn't natively report on memory usage. To understand why, let's visualize what I like to think of as the virtualization layer cake. The bottom layer of the cake represents the physical servers that AWS designs, builds, and operates. On top of that physical layer, AWS installs a Hypervisor. That Hypervisor is responsible for allocating physical resources to virtual EC2 machines. On top of the Hypervisor is where EC2 [[Virtual Machines]] or instances get installed. It is the job of the Hypervisor to map processor, memory, disk, and network requests from EC2 to the actual [[Hardware]] on which the instance is running. CloudWatch has access to metrics which are visible to the Hypervisor. However, CloudWatch doesn't have visibility into what's happening inside the operating system
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/extending-cloudwatch?u=76281980&t=94)** on an EC2 instance. Take memory as an example. The Hypervisor allocates each EC2 instance a contiguous chunk of memory, then the operating systems running on EC2 manage memory based on application needs. Since the Hypervisor doesn't know how each guest operating system is managing memory, CloudWatch can't report on memory utilization. The only way to get memory information is to install a software agent inside of EC2. On the guest operating system, this agent can get memory metrics directly from the operating system, then report it to CloudWatch. Of course, software agents aren't limited to memory reporting. Software agents can collect other application-specific metrics you might be interested in. For example, if your application uses [[Java]], you may be interested in collecting Java heap usage or the number of active threads. All you need to do is write an agent to collect that information, then push it to the appropriate CloudWatch API. Of course, the software agent will need permissions to interact with the CloudWatch API. From an operational perspective, the best way to grant EC2 instances permissions to report any custom metric to CloudWatch is by attaching an identity and access management role
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/extending-cloudwatch?u=76281980&t=189)** to the EC2 instance. By using an IAM role, you can prevent credential sprawl, and avoid embedding credentials on the EC2 instance. Remember, you can assign an IAM role to the EC2 instance while it's running. Depending on the metrics that are important to monitoring the health of your application, I'm sure you can appreciate why it's sometimes necessary to install a software agent to shovel those metrics into CloudWatch.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Amazon EC2|Ec2]] (12), [[Java]] (2), [[Virtual Machines]] (1), [[Hardware]] (1)
> **Env Vars:** ec2 (12), aws (3), api (2), iam (2)
> **CLI Commands:** aws (3)
> **Prerequisites:** install (2)
> **Analogies:** for example (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

The first step in this role challenge is to create a new role called Custom [[Amazon EC2|EC2]] CloudWatch. The intent of this role is to allow EC2 to send metrics to CloudWatch. #### [Understanding Security Token Service](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-security-token-service?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-security-token-service?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] The [[AWS Security]] Token Service, or STS, is a powerful tool you can use for granting temporary permissions to resources in your AWS account. Let's explore some of what can be accomplished using STS. The Security Token Service is useful for granting temporary access to resources within an AWS account. This temporary access can be given to IAM users within the same account. Temporary access can also be granted to federated enterprise or web identity users. STS can also be used to provide access across multiple AWS accounts. To illustrate, let's explore a use case that can be fulfilled using an IAM role that grants IAM users elevated access in the same account. Madeline and Enzo represent our senior most engineers. Recall that they're both assigned to the engineering admin group, which gives them full VPC access as well as full [[Amazon EC2|EC2]] access. It also gives them comprehensive read only access to resources in this account like RDS. Now, let's suppose one of our development, [[MySQL]] RDS instances needs to be rebooted and our database administrators are currently unavailable. In order to allow Madeline or Enzo to perform that reboot,
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-security-token-service?u=76281980&t=93)** we can create an IAM policy that allows STS access. Then we can create an IAM role that has permissions on the same account and grant that role full access to RDS. After attaching that role to the engineering admin group, Madeline or Enzo could elevate their permissions temporarily and perform the reboot.

#### [Illustrating access restrictions](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/illustrating-access-restrictions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/illustrating-access-restrictions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] As you use IAM to grant access to resources, there may be some actions you want to implicitly deny for all IAM users in your account. Consider the following scenario, which I've set up in my account. Recall that we have a user, Madeline. Per the Super Admin Challenge, she can now assume the Super Admin role. Recall that this role contains the AWS Managed Policy for Administrator Access. Looking at the contents of this policy, recall that it allows any action on any resource. With this broad level of access, we might want to put reasonable restrictions in place to support separation of duties. For example, CloudTrail is where audit logs of API activity is stored. As such, it's a good idea to inhibit the ability to delete CloudTrail artifacts. The Cloud [[Hardware]] Security Module is a means for storing cryptographic keys. If you decide to use the key management service instead of cloud HSM, you may want to inhibit the ability to use this service. Clearly, you are using IAM to manage users. However, do you want to let people turn off multifactor authentication, remove an IAM user or delete your password policy? If not, you will want to inhibit that access as well.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/illustrating-access-restrictions?u=76281980&t=98)** I've gone ahead and created a policy called global-deny, which restricts access to CloudTrail, Cloud HSM, and certain IAM permissions. As you can imagine, things can get rather complicated. In order to help you explore the effects of your policies, AWS has made a Policy Simulator.

>

> **CLI Commands:** aws (2)
