#### [Exploring IAM policy simulator](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/exploring-iam-policy-simulator?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/exploring-iam-policy-simulator?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Now that we know that the IAM policy simulator exists, let's dive into the console and see how it works. Here I am at the IAM dashboard. Let's take a quick look at our existing roles by clicking on the roles link under access management. The role I'm interested in is our super admin role. I filter for, then select the super admin role. On the summary page, I can see the permissions attached to this role. As described, it has comprehensive access to everything. We can simulate the effects of this policy using the IAM policy simulator. Clicking the simulate button opens the policy simulator in a new window. Note that it defaults to users. For example, I know that Madeline is in the engineering admin group. If I want to simulate her user, I can simply select her. The resulting screen shows me the policies associated with her user. Here I can see the temp DBA, temp super admin, read only access, [[Amazon EC2|EC2]] full access, the ability to change her password, and VPC full access. With this knowledge, let's see what she can do inside of CloudTrail. On the right hand of the screen, from the select service dropdown, I specify CloudTrail. Note that I can identify individual CloudTrail actions.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/exploring-iam-policy-simulator?u=76281980&t=98)** In this case, I just want to simulate all actions, so I click the select all button. To see what Madeline can do, I go ahead and press the run [[Simulation]] button. Okay, the results look pretty good to me. She can't create a trail, delete a trail, or delete an event data store. But wait a second, that doesn't exactly make sense. Understanding why requires closer examination. Expanding the delete trail, we can see that the permission is implicitly denied. There's no matching statement. How can that be? It's because the policy simulator doesn't follow roles that can be assumed. To simulate what the super admin role can do, I click the back button. Instead of simulating a user, I select roles from the dropdown. Then I filter to my super admin role and select it. Now that I'm looking at the role appropriately, I'm going to rerun the simulation. As expected, all actions within CloudTrail are now allowed. Let's go ahead and address that by attaching the global deny policy to this role. Closing the policy simulator returns me to the previous tab with details about the super admin role. What I want to do is add permissions to that role. The policy I'm looking for is my global deny policy.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/exploring-iam-policy-simulator?u=76281980&t=199)** Expanding it, we can see from the [[JSON]], some of the things that it does. From an IAM standpoint, it inhibits the ability to detach MFA or delete anything. In the cloud HSM section, all activity is denied. From a CloudTrail standpoint, the ability to delete an event data store, delete a channel, delete a resource policy, service linked channel, or a trail are all denied. Since that looks good to me, I go ahead and click the add permissions button. Now these restrictive policies have been applied to this role. And remember, an implicit deny always wins. To see the effects of these changes, I go ahead and click the simulate button once again. This pops open the policy simulator. Opening the dropdown, I select roles, then filter for super admin. In terms of the service, I select CloudTrail once again. Selecting all actions, I proceed to run the simulation. Looking at the simulation results, I can see that the ability to delete things within CloudTrail has been restricted. I can expand the delete trail deny message
>
> **[4:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/exploring-iam-policy-simulator?u=76281980&t=295)** to see why that's true. In this case, I can see that it's due to a statement in the global deny IAM policy. Note that it's possible to test attaching and detaching policies. For example, if I detach the global deny and rerun the simulation, I see that all actions within CloudTrail are allowed. However, you should be aware that when you're working in the policy simulator, you're not actually making changes to any policy attachments in the console. Switching back to the summary page for our super admin role, if we refresh the page, we see that the global deny policy is still attached. Returning to the policy simulator tab, it's worth reiterating that when you simulate any activity for an individual IAM user, any role that they can assume is not included in the simulation. It's just something to be wary of when debugging. So for example, returning to Madeline, you would have to understand the IAM policies that are associated with her, and then understand if any of those can assume an additional role. If additional roles are assumed, you need to navigate back, examine the role, and make sure that you understand the implications of the role itself. I'm confident you can appreciate
>
> **[6:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/exploring-iam-policy-simulator?u=76281980&t=389)** how non-trivial IAM policies can be. The policy simulator really can be your best friend when troubleshooting permissions in an AWS account. Each organization you'll encounter will be different. Take plenty of time to study in depth the actions you want to inhibit in your account. Once you have your list, consider implementing a global deny policy, keeping in mind that any given user or role can have at maximum 10 policies attached to it. In order to minimize disruption to your users, you need to carefully consider when you make changes to your IAM policies. Making changes in the middle of the day can have unintended consequences on your users. While it's a good idea to test these changes off hours, it's a better idea to test them in a completely separate AWS account that you use for policy development.

#### [Understanding Cognito](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-cognito?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-cognito?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Amazon Cognito is a service that makes adding access control to a web or mobile application easy. Let's examine why you might want to use Cognito to reduce the complexity in one of your applications. Let's say you are building a new web application. You want to maximize the percentage of time you put into developing the functionality of your application. However, you also may want to manage how users interact with your application. Most likely, you need users to register for accounts with your site. You may want to store custom attributes about each user, such as their name or date of birth. Once they have an account, you need the ability to authenticate each individual. If you're serious about security, you certainly want to incorporate multifactor authentication as part of your [[User Management]] approach. You'll also want to authorize different people to have different levels of access within your application. Most likely, you'll want to create groups of users. That way, you are granting access to collections of people, not individuals. You'll also want to address a broad range of account management features, such as self-service password reset, and disabling or deleting accounts. Building user management capabilities on your own
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-cognito?u=76281980&t=94)** is time consuming. Fortunately, Cognito is one way to accelerate this process. Let's explore some Cognito concepts that make all of this possible. Within Cognito, you create user pools. User pools let you manage users based on identity source. For example, if you want to manage users independently within your application, you can create a Cognito user pool. However, if you'd like to use a social identity provider, you can create a federated user pool. With a federated pool, you can use providers including Apple, [[Facebook]], and [[Google]] as a source of identity. Basically, if you have an identity source that supports the security assertion markup language, or OpenID Connect, you can federate a user pool with that source. In addition to user pools, you also have the option of creating identity pools. Identity pools allow you to map user pools to different permissions. There are two basic types of identity pools, authenticated and guest access. With authenticated pools, you can map user pools to certain IAM permissions, while guest access lets you restrict users to only the features of your application that are available to anyone with an internet connection. As is the hallmark of AWS offerings,
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-cognito?u=76281980&t=189)** Cognito is built with security as a centerpiece. This includes multifactor authentication support, as well as encryption of data in transit and at [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|rest]]. Cognito is also highly scalable, capable of supporting millions of users. Cognito integrates with a variety of other AWS components, including identity and access management, Lambda, and API Gateway. This ability to easily integrate with other AWS services lets you craft applications by blending together a variety of different components. From a look and feel standpoint, Cognito is easily customizable. The overall look and feel of what users see, as well as how they sign up and sign in are all configurable.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>

> **Env Vars:** aws (3), iam (1), api (1)
> **CLI Commands:** aws (3), make (1)
> **Analogies:** such as (2), for example (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)

#### [Understanding Verified Permissions](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-verified-permissions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-verified-permissions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The era of zero trust application design is here, requiring applications and users to prove their identities before accessing data. Managing fine-grained permissions in a zero trust architecture at scale is challenging for application builders, and Amazon Verified Permissions helps to ease that burden. Traditionally, developers embed permissions into application code. Suppose you are building a help desk application and need your help desk representatives to adjust user quotas. In this scenario, let's suppose that Ramandeep is an application user who is part of the help desk team. Meanwhile, Anushka is an application user who is one of your customers. Historically, you would have to use application logic to verify Ramandeep, his group association, and the intended action before allowing him to make any configuration changes. Meanwhile, if Anushka tried to make a quota adjustment, your application logic would have to handle that effectively. Let's examine how Verified Permissions can help work around the embedded authorization problem. Instead of writing application code, you use the open source Cedar policy language to create authorization policies. With Cedar, you can define role-based access control policies.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-verified-permissions?u=76281980&t=95)** For example, you could use a person's team affiliation to grant access. Alternatively, you can implement attribute-based access control if you have even more granular permissions needs. For example, you could prevent help desk employees from making any application changes outside their normal working hours. Verified Permissions can use a variety of identity providers, including Amazon Cognito, as well as external sources like Okta. The beauty of Verified Permissions is that Cedar policies are evaluated at runtime by a central policy engine. This separates policy definition from [[Software Development]]. Returning to our help desk application scenario, we still have Ramandeep in the help desk and Anushka as a customer. Instead of using application code to grant authorizations, we can simply substitute a Cedar policy document. Embracing the concept of default deny as user requests filter through the runtime evaluation engine, the role-appropriate action is permitted. Another great thing about Verified Permissions is that it allows you to write policies that conform to use cases. If access requests come from people, other systems, or through an API call, the Cedar policy evaluation engine
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-verified-permissions?u=76281980&t=191)** will evaluate centrally-defined policies and allow the appropriate action. With Verified Permissions, you end up abstracting authorizations from code. In addition to letting you think more holistically about how your application is accessed, Verified Permissions keeps all of your policy definitions in a central location. This central policy location and its associated runtime engine improves the consistency of how access requests are authorized. This makes Verified Permissions a real asset when it's time for that inevitable access audit.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Software Development]] (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (2)
> **Definitions:** is an  (2)
> **Analogies:** for example (2)
> **Env Vars:** api (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### [Understanding federated access](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-federated-access?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-federated-access?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Allowing non-AWS users the ability to access resources in your AWS account is an important capability to consider when planning your organization's approach to user security. The modern business integrates many different applications to maintain employee productivity. If you have an existing identity management system, you may want to grant people from your existing authentication store delegated access to your AWS account. Let's explore how to set up federated access. Federated, or externally authenticated, users can come from a variety of sources. There are many external entities you may want to federate with. For instance, you may have an on-premises authentication system, like a local [[Microsoft]] [[Active Directory]]. You may want to federate with other AWS accounts that you own or those belonging to partners you work with. Using a web identity provider, such as Amazon Cognito, Meta's [[Facebook]], or [[Google]], is a common use case for federation. Basically, if an identity provider supports OpenID Connect or the Security Assertion Markup Language, commonly called SAML, you can federate it with AWS. Let's look at a typical use case where federated authentication applies. Suppose you have an employee that works in finance.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-federated-access?u=76281980&t=94)** Every day, Allison authenticates to her line of business systems using local Microsoft Active Directory credentials. As part of its [[Cloud Strategy]], her company has started using resources in AWS. Congruent with her role, Allison needs to be able to access the finance-related information in the AWS console. Allison's company does not want to provision her with an AWS IAM user, as it would require her to remember separate credentials and increase the management overhead. Instead, her company federates its on-premises Active Directory and maps Allison's Active Directory user to the AWS IAM role that has the permissions to view the billing details of the AWS account. Active Directory remains the source of truth for authentication, while authorization is done in AWS by mapping AD users to IAM roles.

>

> **Env Vars:** aws (11), iam (3), saml (1)
> **CLI Commands:** aws (11)
> **Definitions:** is an  (1), is a  (1)
> **Analogies:** for instance (1), such as (1)
> **Tools:** aws console (1)
> **Prerequisites:** set up (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### [Securing financial access](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/securing-financial-access?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/securing-financial-access?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] At this point, you're familiar with the IAM controls that can limit what engineers are able to do within your AWS account. While engineering is important, let's not forget our friends in finance. The financial view of your AWS account is an important one, and so is controlling access to those details. Let's explore how to configure IAM users to manage the financial aspects of your AWS account. Contained within the Billing and [[Cost Management]] dashboard, AWS offers a rich set of tools to help you understand the financial health of your account. Some of the most important features of the Billing and Cost Management dashboard include the Billing and Payment section. In this section, you can view month to date spend, review historical payments, specify the details of how you pay for AWS every month, and apply AWS-granted credits to your account. The Cost Analysis section lets you interactively explore your costs using Cost Explorer. This gives you a detailed perspective of your spend by each AWS service offering. You can also export your cost data for further analysis. The Cost Organization section lets you create cost categories across multiple AWS accounts. The Budgets and Planning section lets you create target budgets for your AWS service costs on a monthly basis.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/securing-financial-access?u=76281980&t=95)** It also lets you see how you are performing against those budget targets. This is particularly useful if you are with a large organization and have multiple accounts for different divisions. The savings and commitment section provides recommendations on how to optimize your costs, as well as manage any savings plans or instance reservations you may have. Let's explore two financial management related use cases. Let's focus on two people in your company, Anushka and Satish. Anushka is a finance power user. She needs to be able to access every financial tool AWS makes available. From remitting payment to cost analysis to budgeting, Anushka does it all. She will need to be in an IAM group that has the permissions on all billing features. That said, she doesn't need to manipulate any of the technical services AWS provides. Meanwhile, Satish is an engineering manager. Satish and his team rely on a number of AWS technical tools to deliver the services that they are responsible for. In addition to using AWS technical tools on a daily basis, Satish is also accountable for his team's budget. To that end, he needs visibility into his AWS costs. While Satish does need the ability
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/securing-financial-access?u=76281980&t=191)** to explore and visualize his costs, he doesn't need the same access that Anushka does. For example, Satish will never be asked to remit payment against the account. Recall that Satish has system administrator access to AWS. We want to make sure that he does not have the ability to make changes as to how billing is handled.

### 3. Multi-Account Access

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Understanding Organizations](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-organizations?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-organizations?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] AWS organizations is a tool that lets you centrally manage multiple AWS accounts. It is critically important to understand the security controls that are available within an AWS account. That said, as your use of AWS grows increasingly sophisticated, it is likely that you'll end up in a multi account environment. With organizations, you identify a master account, then link multiple member accounts to the master. This lets you perform administrative functions and manage costs centrally. For managing security controls, you can use service control policies or SCPs. With an SCP, you can restrict access to regions and set maximum permissions for member accounts. As is the case with most AWS services, organizations are API-enabled. That means you can create a member account programmatically, as well as put account limitations in place with SCPs. If you end up managing a large distributed environment, you can group similar member accounts together in organizational units. Consider the following scenario. You select one of your AWS accounts and designate it as the root account of your organization. Now suppose you have an account in which research and development takes place.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-organizations?u=76281980&t=95)** You can make that account a member account so finances can be managed centrally. Then suppose you decide to segment out the public facing applications you provide into accounts of their own. If you have certain security policies you want every application to adhere to, you can group those accounts into an organizational unit. This becomes especially useful when operating at scale as you can project security policies at the OU level and the member accounts inherit those policies. I'm sure you appreciate how organizations can give you confidence in reducing the variability of your configurations when managing multiple AWS accounts.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Env Vars:** aws (7), scp (1), api (1)

#### [Understanding Control Tower](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-control-tower?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-control-tower?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The needs of a modern enterprise typically go beyond a single AWS account. In order to facilitate management of multiple accounts, AWS created Control Tower. Control Tower offers a number of features that make managing multiple accounts easier, more consistent, and more secure. The first concept to understand is that of a Landing Zone. A Landing Zone lets you develop blueprints for ensuring that you use best practices across all of your accounts. For example, you can ensure that federated access and centralized logging are set up within a Landing Zone. Incorporating configurations from your Landing Zone is your Account Factory. An Account Factory is a tool within Control Tower that lets you provision additional AWS accounts based on configurations that you specify. This lets you spin up entire accounts that follow your security policies with ease. When specifying accounts, you also configure Guardrails. A Guardrail consists of rules that helps you with your security and compliance obligations. As the name implies, Guardrails are rules that will help the people within your accounts do the right things while preventing changes that are potentially destructive or non-compliant. For example, a mandatory Guardrail
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-control-tower?u=76281980&t=93)** inhibits changing any IAM roles in a managed account if the role was created from Control Tower. Guardrails can be preventative in nature. For example, you may want to limit permissions using service control policies. They can also be a detective, for example, enforcing config to detect changes in an account. Finally, Control Tower provides a dashboard to give you a bird's eye view of what's happening across your AWS environment. This lets you do things like view the number of organizational units, accounts, and Guardrails in your environment. It also gives you visibility into anything that isn't compliant with the Guardrails that you've established. From a cost standpoint, Control Tower is available at no additional charge/ While you, of course, pay for the underlying AWS resources that you're using in your environments, Control Tower simply improves your ability to manage how those resources are being used, positioning you well from a security and compliance perspective.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>

#### [Understanding IAM Identity Center](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-iam-identity-center?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-iam-identity-center?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Presenter] Now that we have a good grasp of identity and access management concepts, let's see how it's possible to centrally manage access to multiple accounts with Identity Center. IAM Identity Center is a service design to centralize your workforce's access to web applications and AWS accounts. Within AWS, you may be accustomed to provisioning permissions to individual IAM users. However, with Identity Center, you can centrally define permissions and grant them to Identity Center users across multiple AWS accounts. If you work with an organization with an existing identity source, like [[Microsoft]] [[Active Directory]], you can use that identity source to map your existing users to users within Identity Center. Conceptually, this is similar to federating external access with IAM Users. Identity Center is not just for managing access to AWS accounts. It works with applications that use the Security Assertion Markup Language or SAML for authentication. Identity Center improves the authentication experience for people since Identity Center provides a user portal that prevents users with their authorized applications. Just as an authentication portal centralizes access for users, it also makes it easier to audit authentication activity in a central manner.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-iam-identity-center?u=76281980&t=96)** Another wonderful benefit of Identity Center is that you can put time limits on authenticated sessions. Being familiar with IAM concepts, the core Identity Center equivalents will feel natural. Identity Center users are similar to IAM users. The difference is that an IAM user exists in a single AWS account while an Identity Center user can be assigned permissions across multiple accounts and web applications. It's vital to note that an Identity Center user is not an IAM user. Groups within Identity Center are similar to IAM groups. As with users, the core difference is that groups can span accounts and applications. Permission sets govern what groups and users can do. A permission set consists of policies managed by you. Alternatively, you can use policies that are managed by AWS. It's possible to provision centrally-managed, consistent access to multiple AWS accounts from Identity Center, making AWS organizations a natural partner. Let's take an AWS-centric view of how we can use Identity Center to manage permissions. Suppose we have three accounts: development, test, and production. We also have a user, Luca, who needs read-only access to all of these accounts.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-iam-identity-center?u=76281980&t=192)** Instead of creating an IAM user for Luca in each of those accounts, we can accomplish multi-account access more efficiently with Identity Center. After creating his identity center user in a master account, we can assign him to a group. With the group in place, we can associate that group with any AWS account in our organization. As part of the association process, we can apply the appropriate permission set. If we decide at a later date that we no longer want Luca to view the production account, we can simply reconfigure Identity Center and remove his group from the production account.

>

> **Analogies:** similar to (3)

#### [Understanding Resource Access Manager](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-resource-access-manager?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-resource-access-manager?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] AWS Resource Access Manager, or RAM, is a service that lets you securely share AWS resources across accounts. Sharing resources with RAM eliminates the need to provision and manage resources in every account. Conceptually, RAM is a straightforward service. Fundamentally, it makes supported resources accessible from multiple AWS accounts. Resources can vary. For example, one resource might be your customer owned IPv4 address space within the VPC service. Another possibility is to make a clone of an Aurora database that can be useful for moving production data to non-production environments. Depending on the AWS resource you are trying to share, this may be limited to accounts within your AWS organization. Depending on the resource type, you can use identity and access management roles or users to restrict access to the resource you are sharing. For example, access to customer owned IPv4 addresses don't leverage IAM and can only be shared with accounts within your AWS organization. On the other hand, S3 access grants can be shared with IAM users or roles, regardless of whether they are an account within your organization or not. Like most AWS services, you can configure RAM
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-resource-access-manager?u=76281980&t=96)** through the web console command line interface, direct API calls, or a variety of [[Software Development]] kits. You also need to be familiar with a few terms to deepen your understanding of RAM. A resource share is the resource you wish to share, along with who can access the resource and what they can do with it. What they can do with it is defined using managed permissions. The sharing account is the account that owns the resource being shared while the consuming account is the account to which access is granted. Depending on the resource, you may use managed permissions to restrict access to certain AWS principles. In a consuming account, you may also use a resource-based policy to control access. Similar to identity-based policies, resource-based policies are simply IAM policies attached to an AWS resource, like an S3 bucket or KMS encryption key.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Software Development]] (1)
> **Env Vars:** aws (9), ram (5), iam (3), vpc (1), api (1)
> **CLI Commands:** aws (9), make (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (2), similar to (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2)
> **Tools:** command line (1)
> **Prerequisites:** configure (1)
> **Speakers:** - [narrator] (1)

#### [Exploring Resource Access Manager](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/exploring-resource-access-manager?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/exploring-resource-access-manager?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Now that we understand what Resource Access Manager is, let's explore a resource sharing scenario. Suppose we have two AWS accounts, one intended for central administrative use and the other for research. In the administrative account, we have a private certificate authority. Since we don't want to incur the cost or complexity of having multiple private certificate authorities across our environment, what we want to do is let the research account have access to the private CA to issue certificates. Let's get into the console and make this happen. From the main AWS console, I'm simply going to search for RAM. Then click on the resulting link to take me to the RAM dashboard. Here, we can see that there are no resources currently being shared by this account. Since this is our central administrative account, let's go ahead and create the share for that certificate authority by clicking the Create resource share link. The first thing I'll do is provide a name for this shared resource. The next thing we get to specify is the resource itself. Opening up the dropdown list, we can see the currently supported resources that can be shared using RAM, I'm just going to start typing private for private CA to filter then select that service.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/exploring-resource-access-manager?u=76281980&t=99)** The resource I'm interested in sharing is that certificate authority. Selecting the checkbox for the appropriate resource ID, I proceed by clicking Next. Note that under managed permissions, the RAM default permission for certificate authority is preselected. Opening up that dropdown, you can see some other AWS provided managed permissions. While grayed out in this case, for resources that support customer managed permissions, you would have the ability to alter them here. Scrolling down, we can see an overview of the template for this managed permission. Specifically, we can see that the action of issue certificate is included. Meanwhile, the second statement allows the consuming account to get information about the certificate authority itself. Everything looks good here, so I'll go ahead and proceed by clicking Next. First, let's take a peek at what's going on in my private certificate authority. Navigating to private certificate authority shows the default splash screen. #### [Understanding AWS Config](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-aws-config?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-aws-config?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Config is an AWS service that tracks configuration changes to AWS resources over time. Armed with a comprehensive understanding of what is changing in your environment, config is an important tool from a compliance perspective. Let's talk a bit about what config can do. First off, it tracks and maintains a history of configuration changes to resources in AWS. Attaching an internet gateway to a VPC is an example of a configuration change. So is attaching an EBS volume to an [[Amazon EC2|EC2]] instance. Since config tracks these kinds of changes, it allows for an understanding of the relationship between resources over time. For example, you may want to understand which TCP and UDP ports are allowed into a subnet for a particular VPC. If you operate in an environment that spans multiple AWS accounts, you can look for configuration consistencies across accounts. If you decide to leverage AWS systems manager to improve the consistency of your servers, config has the ability to keep track of software configuration changes. This is particularly useful for ensuring operating system patch levels. Overall, config enables you to get an understanding of what changes are happening in your environment over time, where CloudTrail focuses on who is actually making changes. Now that we know a bit about what config does,
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-aws-config?u=76281980&t=96)** let's talk a bit about how it works. Let's say you have a public subnet in a VPC hosting a web application on three EC2 instances. Those instances are fronted by an application load balancer. The load balancer has its own IP address, which abstracts the IPS of the individual servers from your users. In this scenario, none of the individual servers has or needs a public IP address. This makes sense as each server is behind the load balancer. If ever one of those instances gets a public IP address directly, you'd want to know about it. Therefore, you can use AWS config. With config, you can create a rule that checks for a public IP address assigned to an EC2 instance. Config will monitor your environment for EC2 instances that have public IP addresses assigned. Suppose an elastic IP gets inadvertently associated with an existing instance, or perhaps an instance is started in that subnet with a public IP assigned to it. Config will mark that event as non-compliant. All config activity is logged in an S3 bucket for further analysis or more advanced queries. If you want to raise an, you can do that through Amazon's simple notification service, which supports delivery via email, text message, and a large number of third-party integrations,
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-aws-config?u=76281980&t=190)** like [[Slack]]. Ideally, you've already set up CloudWatch so you can get change attribution details. Config will continue to report non-compliance for as long as the defined rule is violated. In this scenario, once the elastic IP is removed, or the instance itself with the public IP is shut down, config returns to a compliance state. There are a number of things to consider when evaluating how it makes sense to use config in your organization. First off, you have to be aware that config rules are cost drivers. The more rules you have, the greater the cost operating in an enterprise situation, it's very likely that you'll be using more than one AWS account. To that end, conformance packs are available to let you apply a given set of config rules across accounts. While config does a great job of keeping track of changes, ensuring you have CloudTrail configured will help you with change attribution. Config does a good job of tracking changes to resources over time. You'll want to carefully consider your requirements around change management and evaluate where and if config slots in. You also have compliance and audit requirements to take into account. With the exception of AWS global services, like IAM, config operations are limited to a specific region. Therefore, you can focus your config efforts
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-aws-config?u=76281980&t=283)** on the regions in which you operate. In the end, I'm sure you can agree that config is a useful tool to maintaining consistency in your operating environment.

It takes time for config to discover the resources in your account.

#### [Understanding AWS GuardDuty](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-aws-guardduty?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-aws-guardduty?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] GuardDuty is a managed threat detection service that continuously monitors your AWS account for anomalous behavior. By coupling machine learning with log data, this AWS service is a crucial part of threat identification in your AWS account. There are a number of things about GuardDuty that I'm sure you'll find useful. First off, you should know that GuardDuty operates on a per-region basis. The analysis and reporting that GuardDuty does remains in each region. If you operate in multiple regions or in multiple accounts, you may want to consider aggregating findings using CloudWatch Events. Fortunately, GuardDuty is quite simple to set up, requiring just a single click. Once enabled, GuardDuty continuously monitors CloudTrail, VPC Flow Logs, and AWS DNS logs for behavior that is atypical. That said, you don't have to set up these services for GuardDuty to work, as GuardDuty pulls data from these sources independently. Since GuardDuty effectively runs in the background, there's no impact to the performance of the services you have running in your account. Unlike a product like Systems Manager, there's no agent to install or configure. You'll want to keep the following in mind as you start using GuardDuty. First, the moment you enable GuardDuty is the moment GuardDuty starts scanning activity in your account. Because of this,
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-aws-guardduty?u=76281980&t=92)** historical behavior is not included in analysis. GuardDuty detects events that can be grouped into different categories. For instance, GuardDuty actively looks for signs that your account has been compromised. Suppose you have an unusual geolocation activity from a specific API or from the web console itself. In that event, GuardDuty will raise an alert. As GuardDuty assesses what is normal for your account, a departure from that norm will also trigger an alert. For example, suppose you usually have a couple of T3 instances running. If a number of P2.16XL instances, which are over $14 per hour, crop up, that would trigger an alert. GuardDuty also looks for evidence that an individual [[Amazon EC2|EC2]] instance has been compromised. This can include using atypical network protocols or overall network traffic usage that is out of the ordinary. For example, let's say you're running a web server on a T2 instance. If that instance suddenly starts consuming 100% CPU and initiates an outbound connection on port 8833, the default used by Bitcoin clients, GuardDuty would trigger an alert. Another type of suspicious behavior GuardDuty monitors for is reconnaissance activity in your account. Types of activity in this area include atypical API usage, port scans, and failed authentication requests. For instance,
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-aws-guardduty?u=76281980&t=184)** suppose you have EC2 instances running in two VPCs. If an EC2 instance in one of the VPCs starts probing for open ports in either of the VPCs, that's an indicator that something is amiss. As is true for most paid managed services within AWS, GuardDuty comes with a free 30-day trial. Now, let's get into the console to see exactly how easy it is to enable GuardDuty. Now, let's get into the console to see exactly how easy it is to enable GuardDuty. First, let's make sure I'm in the correct region. Clicking on North Virginia, I want to enable GuardDuty in Oregon, so I click US West Oregon. To enable GuardDuty, I simply search for it in the Services search box, then click the resulting link. This takes me to the GuardDuty homepage. To get going with GuardDuty, I simply click the Get Started button. The subsequent page informs me that GuardDuty sets up a couple of IAM roles to allow it to collect log events. Since that's exactly what I want to do, all I have to do is click the Enable GuardDuty button. That's it. The confirmation message tells me that GuardDuty is up and running, looking after what's happening in the Oregon region.

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/exploring-aws-guardduty?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] With an understanding of what GuardDuty is, let's see an example of the types of alerts it can create. Consider the following scenario. An organization operates services in AWS, primarily in the Oregon region. As such, GuardDuty has been enabled in that region and immediately starts evaluating threats. Shortly thereafter, a hacker, intent on causing mischief, gains access to some AWS credentials. Using Tor in an attempt to mask her identity, she starts poking at the AWS account in question. Let's see what GuardDuty thinks of that. From the console, let's get back to the GuardDuty page. Similarly, if you have a list of known bad actors who are trying to compromise your account, you could add them to the threat IP list. I think you'll agree that GuardDuty is a cost effective way to elevate the overall security posture of an AWS account.

#### [Understand IAM Access Analyzer](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understand-iam-access-analyzer?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understand-iam-access-analyzer?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] One feature of IAM that is very useful for helping in your security efforts is the IAM Access Analyzer. IAM can get quickly complicated in a large distributed environment, making tracking of permissions difficult. Access Analyzer helps deal with that challenge. IAM Access Analyzer is a tool to provide a comprehensive evaluation of permissions within your AWS account for external or unused access. Located within the IAM web console, Access Analyzer can look at S3 bucket configurations, who has access to KMS keys, how SQS queues are configured, what IAM roles do, and who and what they are assigned to, as well as the security profile of Lambda functions. It will also report on any Secrets Manager policies that let an external entity use a secret. Another thing that Access Analyzer can do is inspect unused access to help you work towards least privilege within your account. Let's take a quick look in the console at what Access Analyzer can do. From the IAM dashboard, I select Access Analyzer in the left-hand nav. Since I don't have any existing analyzers, I can get started by clicking the Create Analyzer button.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understand-iam-access-analyzer?u=76281980&t=95)** Before I do so, notice that external access is free, but controlling for unused access can get costly. Just something to keep in mind as you explore this wonderful tool. I'm going to create two analyzers. The first is an external access analyzer. This will look for resources within my account that are externally accessible. If I'm interested, I can see the permissions that Access Analyzer requires to run. Everything looks good here, so I go ahead and click Create Analyzer. Now I'm going to create an Unused Access Analyzer with the understanding that it will cost me per IAM user. Clicking Unused Access in the left-hand nav, I go ahead and click Create Analyzer. One thing that's unique about unused access is that I can specify the tracking period. The tracking period is simply the number of days during which access is evaluated. For demonstration purposes, I'm going to reduce this from 90 days to one day. Scrolling down to the bottom, I proceed by clicking Create Analyzer.

#### [Understanding Amazon Detective](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-amazon-detective?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-amazon-detective?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] AWS provides many Detective controls to help you understand the security happenings in your account. To help streamline your ability to troubleshoot curious security related events, AWS created Amazon Detective. Let's explore how Detective works and how it can help you root cause security related concerns. Although Detective is not free, it does have a tiered pricing model based on the number of gigabytes of logs it ingests. Detective is capable of helping you diagnose security issues across multiple AWS accounts. It's also important to understand that Detective is regional in nature. If you operate in multiple regions and you want to use this service, you'll need to enable Detective in every region in which you operate. While Detective can be used on its own, it works best when connected with guard duty and security hub. Paired with GuardDuty, Detective ingests VPC Flow Logs, CloudTrail, and other Detective tools in the AWS ecosystem to establish threat intelligence in your environment. Using graph theory as a compliment to machine learning, Detective is able to help you understand relationships between events, helping you shorten the time required to root cause a specific event. Detective also incorporates [[Generative AI]]
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-amazon-detective?u=76281980&t=96)** to help understand threats and their potential impacts. As is common practice for many advanced services, there is a 30-day trial for Detective. However, since it takes a couple of weeks to establish a solid baseline, you need to give it some time to learn about what is normal in your account before determining whether or not it's worth it for your organization.

#### [Understanding Amazon Inspector](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-amazon-inspector?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-amazon-inspector?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Amazon Inspector is a vulnerability management tool that constantly scans your AWS environment for software vulnerabilities and network exposure. Let's explore some of the concepts related to Inspector. First off, it's important to understand that Inspector is confined to a single region. It's not available in every region, so it's a good idea to check the current list of currently supported regions to see if it's available where you operate. That said, Inspector does support organizations, so it has the ability to explore resources across multiple accounts. In regions where it is available, Inspector continuously monitors your [[Amazon EC2|EC2]] instances and container images within the Elastic Container Registry for software vulnerabilities and network exposure. Making use of the Systems Manager agent, Inspector's scope expands to include operating system, file system, and application processes. Part of what Inspector provides is a risk score based on comparing your environment with a list of common vulnerabilities and exposures. Let's look a bit deeper into how Inspector works. Consider this architecture where you have users hitting Route 53 for DNS, directed to an application load balancer with an auto-scaled web tier, which, in turn, is linked to a load balanced application tier. Behind that application tier
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-amazon-inspector?u=76281980&t=93)** is an RDS database for persisting data. Inspector implements network-based scans in a noninvasive manner. However, if you want to assess the vulnerabilities specific to your web and application tiers, you need to install the Systems Manager agent on each EC2 instance. Since the agent is installed on the instance, it can see above the hypervisor into the operating system and determine what's running on it. Be aware that the agent will consume some resources on the host instance. Inspector uses rule sets to scope the type of scanning it performs. You can use Inspector to scan for common vulnerabilities and exposures, or CVEs, as cataloged by the MITRE Corporation. Another rule set is based on the Center for Internet Security's operating system security configuration benchmarks for things like [[Windows]] domain controllers and member servers. A third rule set checks for security best practices. For example, on a [[Linux]] system, this rule set will report a finding if any operating system user other than root has write permissions to system directories. Inspector collects data continuously. When an EC2 instance is launched, or when a new package or patch is installed, the affected resources are re-scanned. You can view Inspector results in the web console.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Amazon EC2|Ec2]] (3), [[Windows]] (1), [[Linux]] (1)
> **Env Vars:** ec2 (3), aws (1), dns (1), rds (1), mitre (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2), is an  (1)
> **CLI Commands:** aws (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (1)
> **Warnings:** be aware (1)
> **Prerequisites:** install (1)

#### [Understanding Amazon Security Lake](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-amazon-security-lake?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-amazon-security-lake?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Most enterprises have a variety of operational systems for processing transactions and analyzing data. Maintaining a consistent security perspective across systems can be challenging, and that's where Security Lake can help. As the name implies, Security Lake is a security-centric data lake that facilitates security analysis across the enterprise. As you would expect, Security Lake can collect logs from a number of AWS services, including CloudTrail, VPC, Route 53, S3, Lambda, the Elastic [[Kubernetes]] Service, or EKS, and Web Application Firewall. Security Lake also integrates with Security Hub to aggregate security findings from Config, Firewall Manager, Guard Duty, IAM Access Analyzer, Inspector, Macie, and Systems Manager Patch Manager. By centralizing your security-related logs, you can take a more holistic approach to security incidents. Security Lake leverages the Open [[Cybersecurity]] Schema Framework for security related data. Since it is an open standard, you can create custom integrations to feed data from your own systems into Security Lake. This augments the services
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-amazon-security-lake?u=76281980&t=97)** that Security Lake natively supports, allowing you to have a holistic view of security-related information. Once you have all of your data plumbed into Security Lake, you must specify an AWS roll-up region to store the data, regardless of the region or the system that generates that data. With all your data in a central location, analysts can examine user behavior, respond to incidents, and understand user interactions over time.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Kubernetes]] (1), [[Cybersecurity]] (1)
> **Env Vars:** aws (2), vpc (1), eks (1), iam (1)
> **CLI Commands:** aws (2)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1), is an  (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### [Understanding IoT Device Defender](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-iot-device-defender?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-iot-device-defender?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Garage doors, ovens, doorbells, lights, cameras, and security systems are just a few examples of things that connect to the internet. While configuring, managing, and securing a fleet of internet connected devices can be challenging, [[Internet of Things (IoT)|Internet of Things]] Device [[Microsoft Defender|Defender]] can ease that burden. Device Defender has the capabilities to help you ensure the security of your Internet of Things fleet. One Device Defender capability is Audit. Audit checks your device resources, including X509 certificates, [[IoT]] policies, and client IDs to ensure that they follow security best practices. If any of these standards are violated, Audit identifies them for remediation. Rules Detect lets you configure rules to identify behavior irregularities within your IoT fleet. For example, you can create rules related to authorization failures, the number of TCP connections per device, the number of connection attempts, and more. You can think of ML Detect as an evolution of Rules Detect. Instead of having user-specified rules and thresholds at which an alarm is raised, ML Detect uses machine learning on a rolling 14 day average to identify irregularities and trigger alarms.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-iot-device-defender?u=76281980&t=95)** Note that the machine learning model needs at least 25,000 data points to work, which is an easy number to hit for a large IoT fleet. You can configure Device Defender to raise alerts for non-compliant resources or for detected anomalies. Device Defender can alert using SNS, CloudWatch, and of course the web console. Device Defender also lets you configure mitigation steps in the event of detected anomalies. For example, if Device Defender detects that an X509 certificate on a device is about to expire, you can configure a mitigation action to update the device's certificate. With this brief introduction of Device Defender's capabilities, let's get into the console and explore Audit, Rules Detect, and ML Detect. Device Defender is located within the IoT console. So I can simply navigate to IoT Core. From there, in the left hand navigation, I go to the security area. Here, I have a number of options, from configuring certificates to Audit and Detect. Clicking on the Audit link in the left hand nav, we see that you first have to enable IoT Security Audit
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-iot-device-defender?u=76281980&t=192)** before you can get started with it. I could do that by simply clicking the Automate IoT Security Audit button. Clicking on the View Scheduled Audit, we can see the checks that will be performed on a daily basis. In this area, I can simply go to the Audit section in the left hand navigation, then click Results. Here, I have the results of an on-demand scan that I ran, as well as the results of a Daily Defender Audit. Clicking on the on-demand link and scrolling down, I can see that one of the compliance checks which has triggered is the fact that logging is disabled for this environment. Clicking on the Logging Disabled link brings me to a page with additional details. From here, I could follow the Account Settings link if I wanted to remediate this finding. Now let's take a quick peek at the Detect area. Clicking on Detect, notice that I have a couple of options, the ability to use machine learning to detect anomalies, or the ability to define rules myself. Let's see what it looks like
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-iot-device-defender?u=76281980&t=285)** to go ahead and define a rule yourself. Clicking the Define Rules link, the first thing I need to do is specify the name for the security profile. In this case, I'll just specify Authorization Failure. The next thing I can specify is which IoT devices this security profile should correspond to. In this case, I'm going to say all things because I want to control for authorization failures regardless of whether the things are registered or not registered. Clicking on the next link brings me to a page where I can select a metric I want to use. Notice there are cloud-side metrics I can choose or device-side metrics. In this case, I'm simply going to go with Authorization Failures. Scrolling down a bit, here's where I define the meat of the rule itself. In this case, if authorization failures exist, that's when I want to be alerted. So I change the dropdown to authorization failures is greater than. Note that I have the ability to choose an absolute number or a relative number. I'm going with absolute in this case.
>
> **[6:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-iot-device-defender?u=76281980&t=379)** If there are more than five authorization failures within a five minute period, I'd consider that to be pretty suspicious and I'd want to know about it. Notice that if I want to add additional metrics, I could do so by clicking the Add Metric link. I'm good with Authorization Failures for now, so I'll remove that blank metric, then click Next. This page gives me the option to export metric data to IoT Core for additional analysis. I'm going to leave that off for now and proceed by clicking Next. I have the option of being notified when this alarm triggers. If I had an existing SNS topic, I could select it from the dropdown list. Alternatively, I could click the link to create a new SNS topic. This opens up the appropriate dialogue in a new browser tab. I'm going to skip notification for now and proceed by clicking Next. On the Review and Create screen, I have the ability to review my metric behaviors, my export settings, and my notification settings. Everything looks good here, so I can proceed by clicking the Create button. Excellent. My rules-based security profile is now in place. If I wanted to create an ML profile, I could do that by dropping down the Create Security Profile dropdown
>
> **[7:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-iot-device-defender?u=76281980&t=472)** and choosing Create ML Anomaly Detect Profile. Here, I will specify a different security profile name.
>
> **[8:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-iot-device-defender?u=76281980&t=505)** Similarly, I'll specify all things, so this security profile will apply to all IoT devices in my account. There's no one right answer in terms of which schedule to pick. It just depends on your specific environment and your needs.

#### [Understanding AWS Elastic Disaster Recovery](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-aws-elastic-disaster-recovery?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-aws-elastic-disaster-recovery?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Elastic Disaster Recovery is a service that lets you easily recover your servers, applications, and [[Databases]]. Taking backups of your existing systems is easy. It's easy to configure a disk snapshot on a periodic basis to protect your account. However, restoring a system from backup and getting it into an operational state can be challenging. That's where Elastic Disaster Recovery comes in. Let's explore a disaster scenario. You operate an application out of US West 2 in Oregon. Under normal circumstances, your users happily interact with your server. One day, a malicious actor manages to install ransomware on your server, jeopardizing the integrity of your data. To bring your server back, you would have to have a process in place, similar to taking snapshots of your volumes, then creating an image from the most recent snapshot that wasn't infected with your ransomware, and finally, launching a replacement [[Amazon EC2|EC2]] instance. Of course, you'd have to take care of any networking configurations to ensure that your new server is accessible to your users. All of that can take a significant amount of time and effort. Elastic Disaster Recovery can help simplify the process, reduce the complexity,
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-aws-elastic-disaster-recovery?u=76281980&t=93)** and shrink the amount of time required. How does Elastic Disaster Recovery do it? When you enable this service, one of the first things you configure is a replication server. The job of the replication server is to continuously replicate the data and configurations of your existing servers, whether they are on-premises in a different cloud provider, or within AWS. The replication server manages all of the copying and snapshotting activity. In the event of a disaster, EDR facilitates near realtime recovery, minimizing downtime and data loss by maintaining up-to-date copies of your systems on the AWS Cloud. You simply select from the available recovery times and EDR handles rebuilding the server. One of the best things about Elastic Disaster Recovery is that you can simulate service restoration using a recovery drill. When you run a recovery drill within AWS, you first select the point in time you wish to recover from. EDR automatically creates a replacement server. It's also possible to configure post-launch checks to ensure that everything is as you expect once the server is up and running. After validating that the new server has come up cleanly, it is automatically terminated.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Databases]] (1), [[Amazon EC2|Ec2]] (1)
> **Env Vars:** aws (3), edr (3), ec2 (1)
> **Prerequisites:** configure (3), install (1)
> **CLI Commands:** aws (3)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2)
> **UI Navigation:** select the (1)
> **Analogies:** similar to (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

I think you'll agree that navigating the Elastic Disaster Recovery console is much more straightforward and less complicated than doing all of the recovery steps manually. EDR is a true ally when it comes to facilitating recovery.

#### [Removing a source server](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/removing-a-source-server?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/removing-a-source-server?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] Inevitably, you may wish to remove a server that's being protected by Elastic Disaster Recovery. Fortunately, it's a pretty straightforward process. Within the Source servers subsection of the Elastic Disaster Recovery Console, the first thing you need to do is select the server you want to remove from protection. The first thing you need to do is under Actions, disconnect it from AWS. Immediately, you're prompted with a modal window warning you that all associated backups will be discarded. Since that's exactly what I want to do, I'm going to go ahead and click Disconnect. Immediately, I get the confirmation message that this source server was disconnected. Now I can go ahead, select that server, go to Actions, and choose Delete server. This is equivalent to terminating a server. Since that's exactly what I want to do, I digest the message written in this modal window, then, click Permanently delete. That's all there is to it. That server is no longer being protected by Elastic Disaster Recovery.

### 5. Network and Application Protection

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Understand Web Application Firewall](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understand-web-application-firewall?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understand-web-application-firewall?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Web application firewall, or WAF, is a service that helps you protect your web applications from malicious attacks. If you operate public facing services, WAF is a service you'll want to know quite a bit about. At a high level, WAF offers three main types of protection. The first is protection against distributed denial of service, or DDoS, attacks. Commonly presenting as floods of HTTP traffic, DDoS attacks can generate sufficient workload to knock a website offline. Web application attacks typically seek to compromise the code that you're running and can include techniques such as [[SQL]] injection and server side request forgery. WAF's bot control capability helps you identify bad robot networks that are seeking to abuse your systems. As you get started with WAF, you're going to want to understand a bit more about what it can do and how it works. WAF is a managed firewall that helps protect your web applications by working in conjunction with other AWS tools. CloudFront distributions and application load balancers can be protected with WAF. If you use the AWS API Gateway service to publish APIs to the world, you'll be happy to know that WAF integration can help protect your APIs. Similarly, if you use AppSync to expose a [[GraphQL]] endpoint,
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understand-web-application-firewall?u=76281980&t=98)** WAF can protect that as well. In order to provide that protection, WAF uses web access control lists or ACLs. A web ACL is a collection of rules used for filtering web requests. There is a soft limit of 100 ACLs per region, and each one has a hard limit capacity of 5,000 web ACL units. To alleviate many common concerns, AWS provides a set of actively managed rules that check for common issues. For example, the Open [[Web Application Security]] Project, or [[OWASP]], publishes a top 10 list to help develop more secure software. The OWASP Top 10 is among the managed rules that AWS makes available. It's also possible to author custom rules for your unique needs. For instance, you may want to inhibit traffic from a specific site, or you may want to implement a rule that rate limits requests from a specific IP address. Let's take a look at how WAF operates. Suppose you have a web application in the Ohio region fronted by a load balancer. In a typical configuration, users would interact directly with the load balancer to get to your website. While this is fine for your intended audience, malicious actors may be trying SQL injection
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understand-web-application-firewall?u=76281980&t=193)** or cross-site scripting attacks. To mitigate that threat, you enable WAF. Now, when a request comes in, the load balancer bounces it to WAF. The appropriate web ACL is triggered, and the filtering rules enabled in that web ACL are evaluated. If the inbound request is deemed to be malicious, the request is blocked. If the request is deemed to be innocuous, it gets passed along to the load balancer. With CloudFront, WAF works slightly differently. Suppose you had the same initial configuration and decided to improve end user performance by creating a CloudFront distribution. In this situation, your content can be pushed out to edge locations, like Sao Paulo, Hyderabad, and South Bend. Now, when you enable WAF, the web ACLs and filtering rules occur at the edge locations. As such, regardless of where inbound requests come from, your WAF filters are in play. If you end up using AWS Organizations to manage multiple linked accounts, Firewall Manager enables consistent rule management across those accounts.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[SQL]] (2), [[OWASP]] (2), [[GraphQL]] (1), [[Web Application Security]] (1)
> **Env Vars:** waf (16), aws (5), acl (4), sql (2), owasp (2)
> **CLI Commands:** aws (5)
> **Definitions:** is a  (5)
> **Analogies:** such as (1), for example (1), for instance (1)

#### [Understand AWS Shield](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understand-aws-shield?u=76281980)

Shield is a transparent service that is automatically enabled in every AWS account. In its default state, it offers protection against distributed denial of service attacks. Shield's protection encompasses common DDoS attacks. This includes UDP and TCP SYN floods. If you want to mitigate HTTP GET or POST floods, you'll need to use WAF. One nice thing is that the protection offered by Shield is available at no additional charge. While Shield provides basic protection out of the box, enterprise customers have the option of purchasing Shield Advanced. As the name implies, it extends the protection that comes with Shield. Shield Advanced can help mitigate sophisticated attacks on specific AWS components you configure, such as an elastic IP address, an application or network load balancer, or a resource in Route 53. Another big advantage is that Web Application Firewall is included in the cost of Shield Advanced. This allows you to create as sophisticated a protection scheme as warranted by your enterprise. If you have your own [[Hardware]] behind a Route 53 or CloudFront distribution,

> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understand-aws-shield?u=76281980&t=95)** Shield Advanced can help protect those resources as well. Apart from automated protection, if your organization pays for business or enterprise support, of the biggest advantages to Shield Advanced is access to the Shield Response Team. This team is a group within AWS that responds to DDoS attacks and can help develop protection rules specifically for your organization. This can be an invaluable augmentation of your security team if your organization offers services that attract the attention of malicious actors. While Shield Advanced does offer significant advantages over Shield, it comes at a premium price. Unlike most AWS services, Shield Advanced requires a year-long commitment. Fortunately, if you're using AWS Organizations, the price you pay covers all organizations in your account. Let's jump into the console and check it out. The console for Shield is located in the same area as WAF. Since we were just in WAF, I can get to Shield by simply clicking the Getting Started link under the Shield heading in the left-hand nav. Remember, with organizations, you can centrally manage and project configurations into multiple associated accounts. With Firewall Manager, you can enable a number of security-related controls, from security groups, to Route 53 Resolver DNS firewalls. Let's visualize what this looks like. Let's say you have an organization management account and three child accounts. Let's say that each account is for a specific application, each with its own resources. However, you want to manage security control centrally, so you enable Firewall Manager in your management account. This allows you to push centralized configurations for a number of services out to the child accounts. For example, you can centrally define and distribute web application firewall rules. Firewall Manager can also manage Shield
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-aws-firewall-manager?u=76281980&t=96)** as well as AWS Network firewalls. You can even use Firewall Manager to configure security groups within VPCs in each account. So for example, if you offer web services out of each account, you can centrally ensure a consistent security posture. Firewall Manager also supports Route 53 Resolver DNS firewall configurations where you can manage outbound DNS traffic from your child accounts. Of course, there are also a variety of third-party tools that support Firewall Manager. As you would expect, Firewall Manager integrates with Security Hub, so you can maintain a centralized perspective on compliance. Back in the web console, Firewall Manager is found in the WAF and Shield configuration page. The Getting Started page provides you a perspective into pricing, as well as enumerating the prerequisites for Firewall Manager. If I go down to Settings, I can see that I can't manage Firewall Manager from this account, as it is a child in an AWS organization. >
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-aws-firewall-manager?u=76281980&t=189)** can help you reduce variability by improving the consistency of how you manage security.
