#### [Understanding AWS Network Firewall](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-aws-network-firewall?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-aws-network-firewall?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Presenter] AWS Network Firewall is an AWS service designed to protect VPCs within AWS. Network Firewall is an enterprise level offering that works best when used with resource access manager and firewall manager in an organization with many accounts. In addition to traditional VPC components, like subnets, internet gateways, and route tables, one component specific to Network Firewalls is the firewall itself, which lets you filter traffic for multiple subnets in the same VPC. Network Firewall requires its own subnet where its endpoint will live. Note that you need one firewall subnet for each availability zone in your VPC. As you set up a Network Firewall, be aware that you need to modify the route tables for each subnet in the VPC whose traffic you want to inspect with the Network Firewall. The traffic headed for the internet should flow through the firewall endpoint, which should have the appropriate route table entries to pass traffic onto the internet gateway. Network Firewall has a couple of key components in addition to the firewall itself and its associated endpoints. Firewall policies let you create both stateless
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-aws-network-firewall?u=76281980&t=95)** and stateful rules that define how traffic should be filtered. This is where [[Scalability]] comes into play as a single firewall policy can be assigned to more than one firewall. Firewall policies are examples of the defined ones use many principle. In addition to defining default rules for handling stateless and stateful connections, policies reference rule groups. Each rule group is a collection of rules that mimic traditional firewall rules. Like traditional firewall rules, the rules within a rule group fire in numeric priority order. Rule groups are where scale really comes into play. While you can have up to 20 firewall policies, each with up to 50 rule groups, each rule group can contain 30,000 rules. With each endpoint capable of handling 100 gigabits per second of traffic, you have the tools you need to create centralized abstract rules that meet your complex organizational requirements. In terms of capabilities, Network Firewall lets you perform deep packet inspection on both inbound and outbound VPC traffic. As a stateful firewall, Network Firewall supports protocol detection regardless of the port being used. For example, if you route HTP traffic over port 80 80,
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-aws-network-firewall?u=76281980&t=192)** you can easily create a rule to filter that protocol even though it's on a non-standard port. With rule groups, you can define your own stateless or stateful rules. Usefully, you can also incorporate managed rule groups in your approach. Managed rules are similar to the managed IAM rules in the sense that they're managed by AWS at no cost. There are managed rules available based on domain, IP rule, and threat signature. The intricacies of network firewall could easily consume an entire course. To help you contextualize Network Firewalls and where they sit in the overall security arsenal, if you have straightforward [[Routing]] needs and a relatively simple operational environment, you can use network access control lists within a VPC. However, if your use case includes a complex, multi-account situation, Network Firewall, along with its partners, resource access manager, and firewall manager, are likely just the components your organization needs.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Scalability]] (1), [[Routing]] (1)
> **Env Vars:** vpc (6), aws (4), htp (1), iam (1)
> **CLI Commands:** aws (4)
> **Definitions:** is an  (2), is a  (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (1), similar to (1)
> **Warnings:** note that (1), be aware (1)
> **Ports:** port 80 (1)
> **Prerequisites:** set up (1)

#### [Understand AWS Verified Access](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understand-aws-verified-access?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understand-aws-verified-access?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Today's workforce is increasingly mobile and professionals need access to line of business applications from a variety of non-traditional locations. Historically, accessing remote corporate applications requires a VPN. With AWS verified access, you can lose the VPN while improving your security posture. Consider a typical scenario. Jackson is a salesperson. Like all salespeople, he needs to access the corporate customer relationship management system. Peter is one of Jackson's coworkers in the finance department. To do his job, Peter accesses the corporate enterprise resource planning system. When Jackson and Peter are in the [[Microsoft Office|office]], they can access the systems directly from their assigned corporate IP address. Typically, that works well. However, Jackson needs to visit a potential client in Modena, Italy. While Peter is going on a skiing vacation to Montana, Jackson needs to access the CRM from overseas and Peter's colleagues asked him to take a quick look at some currency arbitrage numbers. To perform their respective tasks, both Jackson and Peter log into their corporate VPN. To simplify location-based access, they can use verified access instead of a VPN. Verified access is a VPC service offering.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understand-aws-verified-access?u=76281980&t=98)** With verified access, you can create access groups and assign granular per application permissions. For example, you could create a sales access group that includes policies for the time and expense, as well as CRM systems. You can create a separate finance access group with policies for the ERP and budgeting systems. You have the freedom to bring your existing identities to verified access. As you would expect, identity center is supported, as are popular identity providers like Okta and Ping Identity. Once in place, you can move towards zero trust access, with Trust evaluated with each request. A major advantage of verified access is the centralization of access logging. You can send logs to S3, CloudWatch, Kinesis Data Firehose, and more. With all of the access logs in a single place, that opens up a variety of possibilities in terms of examining user behavior to further refine your verified access configurations. Verified access relies on some core components to function. The first is the verified access endpoint. To create an endpoint, you need to specify a trust provider like Identity Center, Okta, or Ping. You also need to create a verified access instance
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understand-aws-verified-access?u=76281980&t=196)** and point it to your trust provider. From there, you can create a verified access group. If you have applications that have the same security requirements and user populations, you can put them in the same access group. With that organizational work complete, you apply security constraints to incoming requests by authoring verified access group policies. Using the same open source CR language as verified permissions. You can write a verified access group policy for virtually any use case. This can range from evaluating a request based on an email domain to the looking for a specific patch level on the originating device. For example, you may want Peter to access the budgeting application from his corporate laptop but deny that access from any other device. Verified access is an enterprise class service for managing your complex authentication needs.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Microsoft Office|Office]] (1)

> **Definitions:** is a  (2), is an  (1)

#### [Understanding DNS Firewall](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-dns-firewall?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-dns-firewall?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] AWS Route 53 Resolver DNS Firewall is an AWS service with a really long name that's designed to block DNS queries, giving you the ability to control how DNS queries behave within your VPCs. Suppose you have a VPC with a pair of subnets running a bunch of [[Amazon EC2|EC2]] instances. Unbeknownst to you, one of your EC2 instances gets infected with a piece of malware that uses the command and control DNS communication channels to control and exfiltrate data from the infected machine. Unfortunately for you, this traffic appears as innocuous DNS queries. Fortunately, you can add a defensive layer with Route 53 Resolver DNS Firewall. With DNS Firewall, you can configure rule groups. With a rule group, you can create rules that will allow, deny, or raise an alert when a DNS query matches the domains specified in that group. You can also configure your own list of domains, as well as subscribed-to domain lists managed by AWS. With DNS Firewall in place, you can simply block traffic headed for malicious domains. Another wonderful thing about DNS Firewall is that you can associate it with multiple VPCs. This ability to centralize and scale rules
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-dns-firewall?u=76281980&t=98)** is a real benefit to large organizations. As you would expect, you can use Firewall Manager to control DNS Firewall rules across multiple accounts. DNS firewall is a powerful tool within your security-related arsenal.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Amazon EC2|Ec2]] (2)
> **Env Vars:** dns (12), aws (3), ec2 (2), vpc (1)
> **CLI Commands:** aws (3)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2), is an  (1)
> **Prerequisites:** configure (2)

I think you'll agree that DNS Firewall is easy to set up and configure and easy to deploy. I'm sure you'll agree it's a nice tool to have to limit outbound DNS queries from within your VPC. Remember, it's never good when trouble is calling from inside the house.

#### [Understanding Amazon Macie](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-amazon-macie?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-amazon-macie?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] Macie is a managed security service that examines S3, and searches for sensitive data. This useful tool can ease data-related compliance burdens that you might be facing. There are a number of things about Macie that I'm sure you'll find useful. Most fundamentally, know that Macie scans S3 buckets for sensitive data. However, Macie is limited in terms of what it scans. Multimedia content including audio, video, and images are ignored. Text files, compressed files, documents, including [[Microsoft Word]] and [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]], as well as PDF, and file formats associated with [[Big Data]], like Parquet, are included in what Macie scans. However, you want to be careful of blindly pointing Macie at all your S3 buckets, as the number of buckets and amount of data scanned in S3 are cost drivers. Similar to S3 pricing, there is a tiered pricing approach based on the number of gigabytes scanned. Please refer to the AWS Macie pricing page for more details. With a somewhat significant cost per gigabyte, Macie's cost model encourages the centralization of sensitive data, and as small a footprint as possible. Regardless of the cost model, this is a sound design principle, as you want to minimize the amount of sensitive data you store, and you want to know where that data is. In addition to AWS tools, Macie can be integrated
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-amazon-macie?u=76281980&t=95)** with a number of third-party security information and event management systems, as well as managed security service providers. I think you'll find Macie a useful tool when it comes to facilitating compliance. For example, if you have to comply with the European Union's general data protection regulation, you'll be glad to know that Macie scans for personally identifiable information. If you're located in the US and deal with healthcare data, you should know that Macie scans for protected health information, and helps you maintain compliance with the Health Insurance Portability and [[Accountability]] Act, or HIPAA, in the US. Beyond person-specific data, Macie also looks for credentials, such as API keys stored in configuration files. Similar to guard duty, you'll find that it's easy to get started with Macie. Let's do that now by getting into the web console. From the main webpage, I simply search for Macie in the services box, and click the resulting link. That brings me to the Macie service homepage. Since I don't have it running, what I see is a bit of a description of the service itself. To get going, I simply click the Get started button. Similar to GuardDuty, Macie needs IAM permissions in order to run. Seeing as there's no alternative, if this is a service you want to use, I'm going to proceed by clicking Enable Macie. Doing so takes me to the Macie summary screen.

#### [Understanding Key Management Service](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-key-management-service?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-key-management-service?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Key Management Service, or KMS, is one of the foundational tools that AWS provides to create and manage encryption keys. Let's explore some of the key, no pun intended, KMS concepts. First off, KMS uses [[Hardware]] security modules to keep your keys safe. From a hardware perspective, the underlying hardware security modules are multi-tenant. They're also compliant with the Federal Information Processing Standard, or FIPS 140-2, U.S. government security standard, which approves cryptographic modules. Changes related to keys are logged in CloudTrail. This will make your life easier from an audit and compliance perspective. Instead of having keys scattered across your organization, KMS allows for centralized management of your encryption keys. KMS supports both symmetric and asymmetric keys for encryption. Whether you want to generate your own keys or want AWS to handle the key generation for you, both options are possible using KMS. As you would expect, KMS integrates with many AWS services. For example, you can use KMS to encrypt bulk data that you're loading into AWS using the Snow Family of [[Microsoft Products|products]]. It can be used to encrypt data at [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|rest]] in S3, as well as both the EBS and EFS block storage offerings.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-key-management-service?u=76281980&t=99)** If you use Aurora for your relational database needs, know that KMS integration comes with it. If you use Certificate Manager to establish the veracity of your [[Web Servers]], you can specify KMS for encryption. Of course, this is just a sampling of the AWS native services that integrate with KMS. KMS also comes with a big operational bonus. If you use AWS-provided KMS keys, they can be configured to rotate automatically. While KMS is not a free service, it is very cost-effective. For current pricing details, please refer to aws.[amazon.com/kms/pricing](https://amazon.com/kms/pricing). By default, every KMS key is region-specific. However, it is possible to replicate a given key across regions, enabling a single key to be used in multiple regions. Let's take a quick look at a use case that would require a multi-region KMS key. For example, the IAM user Madeline is a system operator, Madeline's IAM user, when created, has the ability to operate in any region in AWS unless restrictions have been put in place. Suppose Madeline uses KMS keys to encrypt configuration files which are stored in S3. With her organization's primary operations in Oregon,
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-key-management-service?u=76281980&t=194)** she uses an S3 bucket in that region to hold the encrypted files. For data protection purposes, she uses S3 Cross-Region Replication to copy objects from Oregon to Mumbai. With a multi-region KMS key, she can use the same key directly in Mumbai if she needs to access and decrypt the backups.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Hardware]] (3), [[Microsoft Products|Products]] (1), [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|Rest]] (1), [[Web Servers]] (1)
> **Env Vars:** kms (18), aws (7), iam (2), fips (1), ebs (1)
> **CLI Commands:** aws (8), make (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (2)
> **URLs:** [amazon.com](https://amazon.com) (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### [Creating a KMS key](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/creating-a-kms-key?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/creating-a-kms-key?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] With an understanding of what KMS is, let's get the engineering admin group up and running. Consider the following scenario. Madeline and Enzo are both on the engineering-admin team. In Oregon, they want to encrypt the contents of S3 buckets as well as EBS volumes. After a conversation, Madeline and Enzo determined that the best way to accomplish their encryption goals is to use a symmetric KMS key, created in the Oregon region. In order to create a key, they need the ability to use KMS. So the AWS managed AWS Key Management Service power user policy needs to be attached to the engineering-admin group. By giving both Madeline and Enzo permissions to use the key, they will both be able to encrypt and decrypt objects and volumes using this new engineering admin key. Let's get into the console and make this happen. From the main AWS console, I'm going to go ahead and search for the Key Management Service. Finding it, I click on the link, which takes me to the KMS landing page. To get rolling, I'm going to go ahead and click Create a key. This brings me through the key creation wizard. Based on our use case, I'm going to choose a symmetric key
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/creating-a-kms-key?u=76281980&t=94)** that will allow me to both encrypt and decrypt contents. #### [Automating KMS key rotation](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/automating-kms-key-rotation?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/automating-kms-key-rotation?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Rotating encryption keys is a best practice for data protection. It's also a typical item on an auditor's checklist, so it's something you're going to want to be on top of. Consider the following scenario. In Oregon, you have an S3 bucket and a customer managed KMS encryption key. The buckets default settings have been configured so that every object stored in that bucket uses the encryption key. Over the course of a year, that key is used to encrypt multiple objects as they're placed in the bucket. It's possible to configure the key to rotate automatically on an annual basis. This keeps the same logical key while changing the backing key that is used for encryption and decryption. As year two progresses, every new object placed in the bucket is encrypted with the new KMS key that was created as a function of rotation. However, the original key is not deleted. It is retained. The reason is due to the encrypted objects from year one. If they are requested, the objects get decrypted with the original key. There are a number of considerations to take into account when rotating keys. First, since the alias and the key IDs stay the same, no configuration changes are required
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/automating-kms-key-rotation?u=76281980&t=94)** to use the updated key. However, since the backing key does not get updated, you're essentially storing another key. As such, there is an additional cost. The yearly rotation is applicable to customer managed keys only. AWS managed keys rotate transparently every three years. While automated rotation is convenient, it is also possible to rotate keys manually. However, that is more involved as it requires the generation of a new key, then pointing the alias of the existing key to the new key. Regardless of your rotation approach, be very careful. Once a master key has been deleted, it is impossible to decrypt any data encrypted with that key. Now let's take a peek into the console to see how easy it is to enable automatic rotation. Here I am logged into the Ohio region as Enzo. We can see our engineering admin key. Clicking into this key, our goal is to enable automatic rotation. However, let's examine the regionality tab. Keep in mind that in Ohio, it is a replica key of the primary key which exists in US West 2, Oregon. Automatic rotation must be enabled on the primary key.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/automating-kms-key-rotation?u=76281980&t=189)** When it rotates, the replicas will be updated accordingly. Therefore, let's drop down the region selector and select US West Oregon. Switching regions brings us to the configuration page for this key. Scrolling down, in the regionality tab, we can see that there are two replica keys that reference this primary key. To enable key rotation, I simply click the key rotation tab. At this point, all I have to do is click the automatically rotate this KMS key every year checkbox. Then click save. Immediately I get a message at the top of the screen that the key rotation settings were updated. A year from now, transparent to me and my operations, the backing key used to encrypt new data will be rotated. Meanwhile, data that has been encrypted between now and then will still be available due to the persistence of the original backing key.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Env Vars:** kms (3), aws (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2), is an  (1)
> **CLI Commands:** aws (1)
> **UI Navigation:** checkbox (1)
> **Best Practices:** best practice (1)
> **Warnings:** keep in mind (1)
> **Prerequisites:** configure (1)

#### [Deleting a KMS key](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/deleting-a-kms-key?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/deleting-a-kms-key?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] As with all things that are created, you may eventually want to delete a KMS key. Consider the following scenario. Madeline decides to leave the organization. She also decides to delete the engineering admin KMS key in all regions on the way out. Thankfully, there are safeguards in place, and the key is not immediately deleted. It is merely scheduled for deletion, which will take place a minimum of seven days from the delete request. This is configurable to a maximum of 30 days. When the key is scheduled for deletion, it is possible for another administrator to recover the key, thereby not losing any access to data. Let's simulate this in the console. Here I am, logged into the Oregon region as Madeline. Clicking into our engineering-admin KMS key, I want to go ahead and delete it. Therefore, I go to key actions, schedule key deletion. Note that I get a warning associated with this state change. If I try to enter one day as the waiting period, the web console won't let me. This error is trapped on the front end, and I am prompted to enter a value between seven and 30. Let's go with the minimum waiting period of seven.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/deleting-a-kms-key?u=76281980&t=97)** Then I'll scroll down, confirm I want to make that change, and say, "Schedule deletion." Excellent, I get the confirmation message that this key is scheduled for deletion. Now let's go to the Regionality tab and quickly repeat those steps in Ohio and Mumbai. Clicking on Regionality, I'll open up Ohio in a new tab. Similarly, I'll say key actions, schedule key deletion, seven days, confirm, schedule deletion. After getting the confirmation message, I'll close that window. Then click the link to open up Mumbai. Here, I'll repeat the same steps.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/deleting-a-kms-key?u=76281980&t=152)** Okay, at this point, all copies of the key are scheduled for deletion. In this browser, I'm logged into an [[Amazon EC2|EC2]] instance with an IAM role that enables me to use that KMS key to decrypt objects. Now that that key is scheduled for deletion, let's see what happens if we try and use that key to decrypt a file. The copy command results in an error message. This error message indicates that the KMS key itself is in an invalid state, specifically, that it is pending deletion. Now let's switch to a terminal I have on my laptop. In this view, I've split my screen in two. On the left, I have Madeline's web console where she scheduled the deletion of this key. On the right, I have a terminal on my laptop. On my laptop, I've put together a script that contains a couple of [[CLI]] commands that will reverse this pending deletion. Let's take a quick look at it. This script performs two actions per region where this key exists. The first is a call to cancel key deletion. If we look at the parameters being specified,
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/deleting-a-kms-key?u=76281980&t=247)** the key ID on the right corresponds to the key ID that is pending deletion in the window on the left. Note also that for the first two commands, the region is specified as us-west-2. That's because that's the primary region for this key. Once the deletion request itself has been canceled, the key remains in a disabled state. In order to get back to operational continuity, we need to re-enable the key. That's what the second statement does, enabling the key with that same key ID. We see the script performs these actions first, in Oregon, then in Ohio, and finally, in Mumbai. Let's go ahead and run that script now. Great. It looks like everything worked. Let's get back to our EC2 instance and try that copy command again. Excellent, the success of that copy command verifies that the key is back to operational. If we want to triple check the status of the key, we can simply go back to the console where we're still logged in as Madeline and refresh the page.
>
> **[5:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/deleting-a-kms-key?u=76281980&t=343)** Excellent, note that the status of the key in Mumbai says enabled. Similarly, the status in Ohio says, enabled. This all makes sense, as the key itself was re-enabled. I think you'll agree, it's wonderful that AWS puts these safeguards in place to prevent accidental data loss.

#### [Enabling EBS default encryption](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/enabling-ebs-default-encryption?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/enabling-ebs-default-encryption?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Presenter] Enabling EBS Encryption by default, is a wonderful example of technology that prevents mistakes by making it easy for people to do the correct thing. Consider this scenario, suppose you have sensitive data on an EBS volume, an administrator makes a snapshot of that volume and unintentionally makes that snapshot public. If that volume is unencrypted, any person with an AWS account can make a volume from that public snapshot and access the data it contains. To mitigate against that possibility, you can force encryption for all EBS volumes. Note that this is a regional setting, you have to enable default EBS encryption in each region where you want it to take effect. Once enabled, all new volumes are encrypted by default, similarly, any snapshots of those volumes are affected. From an encryption standpoint, you have the choice of using an AWS managed KMS key or your own customer managed key. Once enabled, there are no exceptions, you cannot create a new unencrypted volume. It is of course possible to turn off default encryption after you've enabled it. It's vital to note that enabling default encryption
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/enabling-ebs-default-encryption?u=76281980&t=97)** doesn't alter any existing volumes or snapshots, encryption only happens on a moment forward basis. Let's take a quick peek in the AWS console and see how easy it is to do this. Here I am at the [[Amazon EC2|EC2]] dashboard in the web console. Enabling default encryption is very straightforward. On the right side of the screen, under account attributes, there is a data protection and security link, clicking that link takes me to a page with a variety of region level controls, for example, with Lifecycle Manager, it's possible to set up a snapshot policy to protect EBS volumes, as well as EBS backed AMIs. Then there's the EBS encryption section, notice that the current state is disabled, that is EBS volumes are not encrypted by default. Scrolling down a bit more, you see that it's possible to ensure that public access for Amazon machine images and elastic block storage snapshots are blocked. While AMI public access is blocked by default, public access for EBS snapshots is currently enabled by default. Scrolling up to the EBS encryption section,
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/enabling-ebs-default-encryption?u=76281980&t=194)** I'm going to proceed by clicking the manage button. To enable default encryption, all I have to do is select the enable checkbox. While the default encryption key is an AWS managed key, note that it's possible to specify a customer managed key here as well. However, I'm going to stick with the AWS managed key, so I simply select it, then click the update EBS encryption button. Immediately I get the message that my settings have been updated successfully. Let's examine the impact of this change. In the left hand navigation under elastic block store, I'll go ahead and click the volumes link. Note that I have a couple of volumes. Scrolling over, I can see that both of these volumes have an encryption status of not encrypted. As I mentioned, enabling default encryption doesn't impact anything that you currently have in your account, it's only moment forward. To demonstrate, let's go ahead and create a volume, I'll do that by clicking the create volume button. Since this is just a demonstration, I'm going to go with the smallest possible size,
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/enabling-ebs-default-encryption?u=76281980&t=288)** then scroll all the way down to the encryption section. Notice that there's a message indicating that encryption is set by default, similarly, the KMS key is the default key that I specified when I enabled EBS encryption. It is of course possible to override that and specify a customer managed key. Scrolling down, I'm going to go ahead and finish creating the volume by clicking the create volume button. On the screen, the volume I just created is the last one on the list. Scrolling all the way to the right, we can see that our new volume has a status of encrypted, we can also see the associated KMS key. scrolling back, now, let's go ahead and take a snapshot of that volume. Selecting it, I go to the actions menu, then click create snapshot. After entering a brief description, I click the create snapshot button. Immediately I get the message that my snapshot has been created. Clicking on the snapshot ID for the snapshot I just created takes me to the summary page for that snapshot. Notice that under encryption its status is encrypted, that makes sense as we were taking a snapshot
>
> **[6:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/enabling-ebs-default-encryption?u=76281980&t=384)** of an encrypted volume. Now let's examine the impact of default encryption on launching a new instance. Clicking on the instances link in the left hand nav, I proceed by clicking the launch instances button. After specifying a name, I scroll all the way down to the storage section. Note that due to our default encryption settings for EBS, the root volume will be encrypted. If you ever wanted to disable encryption within a region for some reason, it's quite simple to do. To demonstrate, I'm going to cancel out of the launch instance wizard, this takes me back to the running instances page. Clicking the EC2 dashboard link in the left hand nav, I return to the data protection and security link under account attributes. From there, it's a simple matter of clicking the manage button for EBS encryption, then deselecting the enable button. With that change made, clicking the update EBS encryption button results in the immediate notification that my settings have been updated. Returning to the volumes link in the left hand nav, let's look at the impact. Scrolling all the way to the right, we can see that the volume which was created
>
> **[7:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/enabling-ebs-default-encryption?u=76281980&t=479)** when default encryption was enabled remains encrypted, as expected the preexisting volumes are not impacted in any way.

#### [Understanding Secrets Manager](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-secrets-manager?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-secrets-manager?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Secrets Manager is an AWS service that allows for full lifecycle management of operational secrets like API Keys and database credentials, backed by Key Management Service or KMS. There are a number of things you should be aware of in terms of Secrets Manager. The first is that it uses KMS behind the scenes for encrypting and decrypting the secrets being stored. You can decide to use an AWS managed key or supply your own customer managed key. Similar to how KMS centralizes encryption keys, Secrets Manager centralizes the management of your operational secrets. Just as you can assign KMS permissions to an IAM policy, the same is possible with Secrets Manager secrets. This incentivizes the centralization of secrets management. Similar to KMS, it is possible to configure secrets to automatically rotate in a manner that is operationally transparent. That said, it is only operationally transparent if you use IAM policies to access your secrets. Since secrets are centralized and their usage is tracked, auditing their use is as simple as going through CloudTrail logs. There are a few other things that you should know about Secrets Manager. First off, similar to KMS keys, Secrets Manager secrets are regional in scope by default. So if you create a secret in the Oregon region, that's the only place it will be.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-secrets-manager?u=76281980&t=94)** However, what if you're using RDS in Oregon with a read replica in Mumbai? Well, in that case, it's possible to configure your secret to replicate across regions. Since Secrets Manager is based on KMS, your destination region has to have the KMS key if you're using a customer managed key to manage your secret. Once you have secrets in place, you can retrieve the value of your secret using the AWS [[CLI]] or an AWS HGTPS endpoint. While Secrets Manager isn't free, the benefits greatly outweigh the nominal per secret cost.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[CLI]] (1)
> **Env Vars:** kms (8), aws (4), iam (2), api (1), rds (1)
> **CLI Commands:** aws (4)
> **Analogies:** similar to (3)
> **Prerequisites:** configure (2)
> **Definitions:** is an  (1)
> **Warnings:** be aware (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### [Deleting a Secrets Manager secret](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/deleteing-a-secrets-manager-secret?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/deleteing-a-secrets-manager-secret?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] As with all things that are created, you may eventually want to delete a Secrets Manager secret. Consider the following scenario. Enzo decides to leave the organization. He also decides to delete the dev-[[PostgreSQL|postgres]] Secrets Manager secret in all regions on the way out, making it impossible to retrieve the current password for the development database. Thankfully, there are safeguards in place and the secret is not immediately deleted. It is merely scheduled for deletion. Similar to a KMS key, Secrets Manager secrets can be deleted a minimum of seven days and maximum of 30 days from the delete request. When the secret is scheduled for deletion, it is possible for another administrator to recover the secret, thereby not losing access to the database. After confirming the region, he clicks Delete replica. With that done, he goes under Actions and chooses Delete secret. Although he might want to delete the secret immediately, he can't. Unfortunately, the approach he took causes a bit of a headache. As my admin user, I navigate over to Secrets Manager. Clicking on Secrets, I see that there are no existing secrets. That's because the default view doesn't show you any secrets that are pending deletion. To adjust that, I click the little gear icon, then click the Show secrets scheduled for deletion checkbox. Clicking Save shows me the secrets that are currently pending deletion. Dev-postgres is the one we're interested in, so I go ahead and select that one. Although it's possible to restore a secret that's marked for deletion, keep in mind that in a multi-region situation, there are some additional complexities involved as the replica keys simply disappear.

#### [Understanding AWS CloudHSM](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-aws-cloudhsm?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-aws-cloudhsm?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] While KMS is a wonderful tool for managing encryption keys, the Cloud [[Hardware]] Security Module offers a greater level of customer control. If your organization requires more flexibility than what KMS offers, CloudHSM provides a viable alternative. Let's explore the compelling advantages CloudHSM provides when compared with KMS. First off, while KMS is based on the shared hardware model that makes the public cloud possible, cloud HSM is delivered via a single-tenant access to a dedicated, highly-available cluster. With KMS, you have the option of creating your own encryption keys or letting AWS do that for you. However, CloudHSM works only with keys that you create. This gives you greater control, as AWS has no access to the keys that you've created. While KMS is FIPS 140-2-compliant, CloudHSM goes even further by being Level 3-compliant. If you run workloads that require that additional level of compliance, the only way to get there in AWS is with CloudHSM. CloudHSM offers a higher degree of security than KMS, and as such, you end up paying for it. Roughly speaking, CloudHSM costs more per hour than a KMS key does for an entire month. Using CloudHSM is a bit more involved than using KMS.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-aws-cloudhsm?u=76281980&t=96)** To get started, you need to have a VPC with at least two subnets. From there, you can create your CloudHSM cluster, which will create a node in each subnet you specify. You then proceed by creating a hardware security module in the cluster. At that point, you download the certificate signing request, sign it, and upload both the cluster certificate and issuing certificate. At that point, you're ready to start creating users and keys. Fortunately, AWS provides command line management utilities so you can accomplish these tasks more easily. CloudHSM offers you a remarkable degree of control over key management at a premium price, and working with CloudHSM is comparatively complicated and involved. Unless you have a demonstrated need for the features CloudHSM offers, KMS is a fine solution for key management.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Hardware]] (3)
> **Env Vars:** kms (10), aws (4), hsm (1), fips (1), vpc (1)
> **CLI Commands:** aws (4), node (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (3)
> **Tools:** command line (1)
> **Exercise Files:** download the (1)

#### [Understanding Systems Manager](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-systems-manager?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-systems-manager?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Systems Manager is a centralized operational hub for managing [[Hybrid Cloud]] resources. While it has many capabilities, I will focus on the security and compliance related considerations. There are a few things that you should know about Systems Manager. First, Systems Manager operates in an agent-based fashion. That means in order for systems manager to control a server, a software agent needs to be installed on that server. Since you can install the agent on resources within and external to AWS, Systems Manager gives you a holistic view as to what's going on in your environment. With the ability to run commands in a distributed fashion, you can use Systems Manager to drive consistency. For example, you can create policies that define a desired state for different server roles, and use the state manager feature to ensure your server configurations comply with your intended state. All of this makes Systems Manager a useful ally in the facilitation of compliance. For example, you can use Systems Manager to coordinate operating system patching and application patching. It's important for folks to anticipate the unexpected. You can use Systems Manager to help system operators know what steps to take when the unexpected does occur. With the Incident Manager feature,
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-systems-manager?u=76281980&t=95)** you can develop [[Incident Response]] playbooks, specifying actions to take and the appropriate escalation procedures. Now let's take a closer look at Parameter Store. Parameter Store is a feature of Systems Manager that operates in a similar fashion to Secrets Manager. It's similar in that it can accommodate KMS backed encrypted secrets. However, Parameter Store is a bit more flexible and supports unencrypted parameters, things like environment variables or application specific settings. Unlike Secrets Manager, there's also a free tier for Parameter Store that lets you store up to 10,000 individual parameters. If you need to store more than 10,000 items, an advanced tier is available at an additional cost. Let's take a closer look at some of the limitations associated with Parameter Store when compared with Secrets Manager. While Secrets Manager allows for the automated rotation of secrets, you need to write your own Lambda function to rotate parameters in Parameter Store. From a scope standpoint, Parameter Store is region specific at the time of this writing. However, just as Secrets Manager has evolved to replicate Secrets across regions, I wouldn't be surprised if Parameter Store eventually accommodates that use case. If you want to share a parameter across accounts,
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-systems-manager?u=76281980&t=189)** you have to accomplish that using IEM roles and trust relationships. There is no explicit way at this time to share a parameter natively across multiple accounts. From a parameter standpoint, if you deal exclusively with things that need to be encrypted and derived value from the ability to automate rotation, Secrets Manager is a great way to go. However, if you have a variety of unencrypted values you need to manage, Parameter Store is a great option.

>

#### [Understanding Incident Manager](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-incident-manager?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-incident-manager?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] If there's one thing that's true, it's that the unexpected will occur. Incident Manager is a component of Systems Manager that helps you prepare for that eventuality. An incident is any disruption from standard operations. Incidents can occur as a result of a server crashing, a software issue, a configuration issue, or any other disruption that impacts service quality. When any of these occur, you can use Incident Manager to help get you back on track. While Incident Manager won't necessarily address the root cause, you can use it to outline the steps that will get you back on the road. Metaphorically, Incident Manager won't fix the broken car in this picture. Within Incident Manager, you configure a response plan. A response plan details the steps to take when the unexpected happens. Following the car crash analogy, a response plan would contain instructions on what to do next: call emergency services, call a towing company, call the insurance company, call a body shop, and arrange for a rental car. A response plan takes the form of a Systems Manager Automation document, which can include both manual and automated steps. This document describes the right people to notify
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-incident-manager?u=76281980&t=96)** and the actions to take. You can also track resolution progress, collaborate as appropriate, and keep the right people informed. After you have resolved the incident, you can review the associated analysis with your team. An analysis document includes a summary of what happened, the timeline of when the incident started, key steps along the way, and when it was ultimately resolved. An analysis document also includes the metrics that detected the incident, any missing metrics that might improve detection, and steps you can take to prevent a similar incident from happening in the future. With Incident Manager, you can be prepared for the fact that in reality, things break all the time. Knowing what to do is essential to maintaining a secure, compliant operational environment.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)
> **Analogies:** picture (1)
> **Prerequisites:** configure (1)
> **Speakers:** - [narrator] (1)

It's also worth noting that once you delete a parameter, it simply disappears. For example, I can simply select all of the parameters in this region and click "Delete". At the modal window, if I click "Delete Parameters," they're simply gone. It's not possible to retrieve them. While automating a secret rotation in Parameter Store is tedious to set up, you must be aware that when a parameter is removed, it's simply irretrievable. Parameter Store is a wonderful ally as long as you understand its limitations.

#### [Understand Certificate Manager](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understand-certificate-manager?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understand-certificate-manager?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Managing and rotating Secure Sockets Layer, Transport Layer Security certificates is a tedious task. AWS makes this process easier with Certificate Manager. Certificate Manager is a managed service for creating and maintaining SSL, TLS certificates. These credentials are critical for encrypting data in transit, as well as verifying the identity of a web server. Certificate Manager lets you manage certificates centrally. This is an important theme as central management and limiting variance equates to consistent processes and stable operations. If you operate public facing services, certificate manager can handle certificates for domains that you own. When requesting a certificate, you have to verify ownership by DNS configuration or email. For DNS, you have to be able to add the C name provided by Certificate Manager. Emails are sent to the domain registrant, technical contact and administrative contact registered in who is. If you want to encrypt private traffic, such as communication with [[IoT]] devices or API Endpoints, you can use Certificate Manager to manage private certificates. It's also possible to export private certificates
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understand-certificate-manager?u=76281980&t=95)** for direct placement on an [[Amazon EC2|EC2]] instance or a server you manage on-premises. Unsurprisingly, Certificate Manager integrates with many AWS services, including CloudFront, application and network load balancers and API gateway. When it comes time to renew certificates, if you added a C name to DNS, Certificate Manager can renew and redeploy public certificates without additional validation of ownership. Private certificate renewal depends on where the certificate is. A managed service like a load balancer, allows for a completely automated renewal. However, if you exported the certificate for placement on a specific resource, it's up to you to deploy the new certificate and private key. Private certificate Authority is the service that enables you to create and maintain private certificates. Private certificate authority allows you to develop and maintain an internal public key infrastructure that leverages certificates for authorization. Since private CA certificates can be revoked, having a private CA improves your security posture when compared to using self-signed certificates. Since private CA supports the ability to have hierarchies five tiers deep, you can have a root CA, three intermediate CAs,
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understand-certificate-manager?u=76281980&t=191)** and one issuing CA. This gives you the flexibility you need to create certificates with layered levels of trust. Another useful feature of private CA is that it supports short-lived certificates with a validity period of seven days or less. Short-lived certificates can be useful for temporary authorization or short duration resource spikes. As part of the certificate manager ecosystem, Private CA also supports a variety of AWS services from EC2 and containers, to on-premises infrastructure. With pricing a minimal concern, let's get into the console and create a private certificate authority.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Amazon EC2|Ec2]] (2), [[IoT]] (1)
> **Env Vars:** aws (3), dns (3), api (2), ec2 (2), ssl (1)
> **CLI Commands:** aws (3)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2), is an  (1)
> **Analogies:** such as (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### [Creating a private certificate authority](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/creating-a-private-certificate-authority?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>

#### [Understanding Payment Cryptography](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-payment-cryptography?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-payment-cryptography?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] If your organization natively processes payment card transactions, you are likely familiar with the [[PCI DSS|payment card industry data security standard]], or [[PCI DSS]]. Designed to protect card holder data, building and operating an encrypted PCI DSS-compliant environment requires a significant amount of effort. Payment [[Cryptography]] is an AWS service that reduces the operational burden of having a PCI DSS-compliant environment. Four primary data elements related to cardholder data are in scope for PCI DSS compliance. One of these elements is the primary account number. If you have a credit card in your wallet, take it out and look at it. If you're holding a Visa or MasterCard, you probably see 16 digits in groups of four. If you're holding an American Express card, you probably see 15 digits. That's the primary account number. Another data element is the card holder name. This is the name of the person to whom the card is issued, and is printed on the card. A third element is the expiration date, and the fourth is the service code, also known as the card verification value, or CVV. To protect sensitive customer information,
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-payment-cryptography?u=76281980&t=96)** PCI DSS requires that card holder data is encrypted. When you buy something with a credit card, the card holder data is provided to the merchant, who, in turn, sends it to a payment processor. The payment processor forwards the details to the acquiring bank, which uses the card network to contact the issuing bank for authorization. PCI DSS requirements require point-to-point encryption of the cardholder data, meaning that it must be encrypted both in transit and at [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|rest]]. If your organization directly takes part in processing card payments, it's probable that you have on-premises equipment with a payment [[Hardware]] security module to handle cryptographic operations. When you own your own equipment, you have all of the associated classic responsibilities like worrying about scaling and hardware maintenance. If you want to remove the need for dedicated payment hardware security modules, you can outsource your cryptographic workload to payment cryptography. Payment cryptography has a number of features and benefits. In terms of key management, payment cryptography uses FIPS 140-2 level 3 validated hardware security modules for secure key storage.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-payment-cryptography?u=76281980&t=193)** Even better, payment cryptography supports automated key rotation to comply with PCI DSS key management requirements. As you would expect of any cryptographic service, payment cryptography has APIs to encrypt and decrypt card data and tokenization support. With tokenization, sensitive card data is replaced with a non-sensitive token, reducing the data exposure risk. Payment cryptography supports essential cryptographic operations, including PIN generation and verification, as well as card verification value calculation. From an access control standpoint, payment cryptography leverages identity and access management to offer fine-grained access control policies. Not even AWS employees have access to your plain text payment keys if you store them in payment cryptography. If you operate additional processing infrastructure within AWS, you'll be delighted to know that you can integrate payment cryptography with a VPC. Crucially, payment cryptography is certified by AWS as a PCI DSS compliant service. As always, the shared responsibility model is in play, meaning if you lose your payment keys
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-payment-cryptography?u=76281980&t=287)** and the means to retrieve them, you are on your own.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Cryptography]] (11), [[PCI DSS]] (8), [[Hardware]] (4), [[Data Security]] (1), [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|Rest]] (1)
> **Env Vars:** pci (8), dss (8), aws (4), cvv (1), fips (1)
> **CLI Commands:** aws (4)
> **Definitions:** is an  (1), known as (1)

#### [Challenge: KMS](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/challenge-kms?u=76281980)

The first step in this challenge is to create a new KMS key in one region. You should be able to use this key for both encrypting and decrypting data. The region doesn't matter. It can be in whichever region you choose. Ensure that you assign a key administrator to an IAM user, and make sure that your key administrator can also use the key. In this case, it's okay to have the same IAM user be the key user and administrator. Once you have created this key, create a second KMS key in a different region. Again, the region doesn't matter. Keep in mind that this should not be a multi-region key, it should be completely separate. Assign a key administrator as well as a user for your second key. Once you've set up your new KMS keys, create an IAM policy. This policy should allow encryption and decryption operations on both keys. Good luck.

### 7. S3 Access Management

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Exploring S3 management options](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/exploring-s3-management-options?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/exploring-s3-management-options?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Simple Storage Service, or S3, is one of the oldest service offerings in the AWS portfolio. S3 provides object storage. With literally trillions of files stored as objects, it's safe to say that S3 is also quite widely used. You can interact with S3 directly via the web console or a variety of available third-party tools. Machines interact with S3 programmatically using the [[CLI]] or using one of the many [[Software Development]] kits provided by AWS. These SDKs are available for many popular programming languages. Machines with an AWS Virtual Private Cloud in your account can interact directly with S3 using a VPC endpoint. It's helpful to understand how S3 is organized. Let's reinforce some AWS specific object-related storage terminology. First off, there's the S3 bucket. It helps to think of a bucket as a folder, a container in which you can store a number of objects. What do you put in a bucket? Well, in S3 terms, you put objects in a bucket. It's helpful to think of objects as files. Objects can be binary files of different types
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/exploring-s3-management-options?u=76281980&t=94)** such as pictures or [[Microsoft Word|word]] processing documents. They can also be simple text files. Essentially, any digital document that you want to store can be considered an object. Objects are placed into buckets. This is analogous to a folder on your desktop or laptop containing various files of differing types. It's important to keep these in your head as it's possible to establish security controls at both the bucket and object levels. Going forward, we'll explore a variety of approaches for managing access to S3. These will include VPC endpoints. VPC endpoints are useful because they keep network traffic between S3 objects and resources within your AWS account private. That is, they don't traverse the public internet. Identity and access management policies are constructs within an AWS account that defines what users can and cannot do. In the context of S3, IAM policies are useful since they can be applied to IAM users, groups, and roles. Of course, roles can be assigned directly to various compute resources like [[Amazon EC2|EC2]] instances. S3 bucket policies are security controls placed at the individual bucket level.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/exploring-s3-management-options?u=76281980&t=188)** S3 ACLs are applied to individual objects within an S3 bucket. Unless you really need to control access to individual objects, it's unlikely that you'll use ACLs as a security control. S3 Access Grants let you map identities from your corporate identity store or from AWS IAM to permissions within S3. Note that you are limited to one instance of S3 Access Grants per AWS region in an account. S3 Access Points let you create a network endpoint for access to S3. You can make these access points publicly accessible, or you can limit them to a specific VPC. Within a single AWS account, it's a good idea to create separate buckets for publicly accessible data, like web content, and privately accessible data, like internal financial documents. That way, if you have users coming from the internet, you can be confident that they'll have access to the data intended to be shared with the world, with your private assets in another bucket, you can [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|rest]] assured that it is protected from the public eye. Depending on how sophisticated your organizational requirements are, you may wish to create separate AWS accounts for public and private data. In addition to ensuring that public users
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/exploring-s3-management-options?u=76281980&t=284)** have the appropriate access while safeguarding your internal assets, this approach also facilitates taking advantage of account level controls for controlling public access. As you can imagine, this can make the structure and management of multiple S3 buckets quite complex.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[CLI]] (1), [[Software Development]] (1), [[Microsoft Word|Word]] (1), [[Amazon EC2|Ec2]] (1), [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|Rest]] (1)
> **Env Vars:** aws (10), vpc (4), iam (3), cli (1), ec2 (1)
> **CLI Commands:** aws (10), make (2)
> **Analogies:** such as (1), analogous to (1), imagine (1)
> **Warnings:** note that (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### [Accessing S3 privately](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/accessing-s3-privately?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/accessing-s3-privately?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] By design, S3 objects are very easy to access using common HTTPS URLs. Communication between any server you operate locally and S3 is routed via the public internet. The same is true for [[Amazon EC2|EC2]] instances you operate within AWS. Let's look at how it's possible to access S3 privately. Suppose you stored a software installation package in S3 and you want to install that software on a local server. From your on-premises server, you could use the AWS Command Line Interface, or [[CLI]], to issue a GetObject call. As parameters to this call, you specify the names of both the S3 bucket and S3 object in your account. The communication is handled via HTTPS and is routed over the public internet. Similarly, the software installation package is downloaded to your server over the public internet. Now, suppose you want to install that same piece of software on some EC2 servers within your AWS account. You issue the same Get request as you did from your local server, and the request gets routed to S3 over the public internet. In order to avoid that configuration, AWS came out with a virtual private cloud endpoint for S3. Recall that a VPC is a networking construct
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/accessing-s3-privately?u=76281980&t=95)** that lets you create a logically isolated network, private, in your account. Within a VPC, it's possible to create an endpoint for S3. This VPC endpoint is a virtual device that lets the interaction between your AWS servers and S3 communicate privately without the need to access the public internet at all. If you create a VPC using the VPC wizard, the default is to create an S3 endpoint, putting an emphasis on that private traffic. Meanwhile, your local server would still use the public internet in order to interact with S3. However, you do have the option to enable private access to S3 from servers outside of AWS. With AWS PrivateLink, you can configure an interface VPC endpoint for S3. This is possible whether you're using a site-to-site VPN to route traffic or are using Direct Connect to get directly on the private AWS network. Once configured, your local equipment can access S3 privately. Using endpoints for S3, you can configure private, secure S3 access for your servers, regardless of if you're running them locally or in AWS. This is true because traffic between the two doesn't go over the internet. Access to S3 through an endpoint
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/accessing-s3-privately?u=76281980&t=189)** can be further restricted to trusted buckets by implementing access policies.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Amazon EC2|Ec2]] (2), [[CLI]] (1)
> **Env Vars:** aws (9), vpc (6), https (2), ec2 (2), cli (1)
> **CLI Commands:** aws (9)
> **Prerequisites:** install (2), configure (2)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2)
> **Tools:** command line (1)

#### [Configuring private S3 access](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/configuring-private-s3-access?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/configuring-private-s3-access?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's take a closer look at configuring private access to S3. There are two options for accessing S3 privately using an endpoint. The first is known as a gateway endpoint, while the second is called an interface endpoint. Regardless of which option you choose, your network traffic stays within AWS. That said, there are some notable differences. With the gateway, your calls use the public IPs for S3, while an interface endpoint uses the private IP addresses. With the gateway option, you can only access the endpoint from within a VPC. However an interface can be configured to be accessed from on-premises equipment, a VPC, or any other cloud provider. A gateway endpoint is limited to a specific region, while an interface can be accessed from multiple regions using the transit gateway, or VPC peering connections, or a global interface. A significant consideration is cost. While there is no charge for a gateway, you do need to pay for an interface. With that overview, let's get into the AWS console and create a gateway endpoint. The first thing I need to do

#### [Managing S3 with IAM](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/managing-s3-with-iam?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/managing-s3-with-iam?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Access to S3 can be managed in a number of different ways. Using identity and access management policies to control access to S3 is a great idea and fits nicely with the best practice of using IAM to control access to AWS resources in general. IAM policies are a great tool for managing S3 access. They allow for virtually any level of granularity imaginable. Suppose the CEO of your company wanted to store confidential documents in S3. You could lock down access to a specific S3 bucket using an IAM policy. That way, only the CEO would be able to access the contents of that bucket. Similarly, if the CEO wanted an S3 bucket to share documents with her leadership team, it's possible to apply an IAM policy to the leadership team's group. More interesting is the ability to apply access policies to IAM roles. Not only can a role be assigned to users and groups, policies can also be assigned to roles. This is a powerful concept. By assigning a policy to a role, that means an [[Amazon EC2|EC2]] instance can be assigned that role and thus the permissions associated with it. Similarly, Lambda functions are assigned roles. The ability to attach a role is a big plus for security, as it removes the need to distribute and manage access keys.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/managing-s3-with-iam?u=76281980&t=96)** Another big benefit to IAM policies is how specific they can be. In terms of S3, you could create a policy that allows full access to one bucket, read-only access to a second, and no access at all to a third. Let's explore some S3 access use cases that can be handled with IAM roles. For example, suppose a company shares files with its customers using S3. Each individual customer has its own S3 bucket. Julia is an employee whose responsibilities include customer interaction. Specifically, she needs to be able to read from and write to the various customer S3 buckets. Within its AWS account, the company uses EC2 instances to generate files for customers. These instances also need to be able to read and write to the customer buckets. In addition, the company stores sensitive information in S3. This information is generated from an EC2 instance. Neither Julia nor the client file generating EC2 instances should be allowed to access this bucket. In the next video, we'll get into the web console and use the [[CLI]] to explore how to satisfy all of these use cases with two IAM policies.

Let's pause and consider the significance of IAM policies in S3. First of all, using an IEM policy with S3 prevents the need for embedded credentials on EC2 instances. Not having embedded credentials saves you from the operational burden of maintaining and rotating access keys. Abstracting Beyond S3, we know that IAM roles can include policies for any AWS service. With that kind of potential, the need to embed credentials on an EC2 instance should be very rare indeed. Second, policies can be simultaneously applied to both instances and individuals, doing so, centralizes permissions management. If you need to change the way a group of people and a group of machines access S3, you can do so by modifying just that single policy. Again, if you think beyond S3,
>
> **[5:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/leveraging-s3-iam-policies-in-ec2?u=76281980&t=317)** you'll quickly realize the power this concept represents. Third, when writing IAM policies for S3, you can handle any complicated permission situation which may arise. You can even write policies to manage multiple S3 buckets with diverse permissions requirements. It's worth reiterating that IAM roles can encompass multiple AWS service offerings. From compute and database to storage and networking, You can design and IAM role and policy combination to fit your organizational needs.
