#### [Creating an S3 bucket policy](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/creating-an-s3-bucket-policy?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/creating-an-s3-bucket-policy?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] S3 bucket policies are security controls applied at the bucket level. Let's explore some defining features and ways in which you can use them to control access that spans multiple AWS accounts. S3 bucket policies are useful in that they specify security controls at the individual bucket level. In addition, they're useful if you use many S3 buckets, each with its own security requirements. Most notably, bucket policies are an ideal mechanism to grant access to a specific bucket across AWS accounts. Perhaps the defining feature of bucket policies is that they allow you to delegate access without sacrificing control. The permissions specified in the bucket policy take precedence over IAM policies. Bucket policies are comprised of four elements, the effect, either allowing or denying an action. Remember, if a deny exists anywhere in a policy, access is denied, regardless of what else the policy says. The principal, which is the Amazon resource name on which you want to control permissions. The action specifies the granular actions to allow or deny. And the resource, which is the ARN. This can get as granular as a single object.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/creating-an-s3-bucket-policy?u=76281980&t=95)** Consider the following use case. A company chooses to separate its development and production systems by implementing separate AWS accounts. For its production systems, the company stores configuration files in an S3 bucket in its production account. Ari is an engineer with an IAM user in the production AWS account. According to her role, she needs to be able to access and modify the configuration files in the S3 bucket. Meanwhile, Rajesh, using his IAM development account, is working on building out a new environment. To get him started, Rajesh needs to be able to reference the production configuration files, but should not be able to update them. Let's go ahead and create a bucket policy in the production AWS account. Here I am in my production account inside of the demo-org-production-config bucket. Looking at the contents, we can see that it contains a variety of nginx configuration files. Now, let's go ahead and look at the permissions tab for this bucket. I do so by clicking on the permissions link. The first thing that's reassuring is that the bucket and the objects within this bucket are not publicly accessible. This is because the Block Public Access setting
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/creating-an-s3-bucket-policy?u=76281980&t=189)** is on for this bucket. That's a good reminder that the bucket and its contents are private, only accessible to authenticated users. Now let's go ahead and craft the bucket policy. Scrolling down to the Bucket Policy section, I click the Edit button. This brings up the policy shell. I'm going to use the Policy Generator to generate the statements for me, as I don't have the [[JSON]] memorized. Clicking on the Policy Generator link opens the Policy Generator in a new tab. Here we can see that a variety of policy types are supported. In this case, I want to specify an S3 bucket policy. Then I need to specify the effect, which is whether I want to allow an action or deny an action. In this case, I want Rajesh to be able to list the contents of this bucket. Therefore, the effect radio button stays at Allow. In terms of the principal, I simply need to retrieve his principal name. Switching to another browser, you can see that I'm signed in as my administrative user. I'm in the identity and access management section that details Rajesh's user information in this research and development account. I'm going to go ahead and copy his ARN.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/creating-an-s3-bucket-policy?u=76281980&t=287)** Note that from an IAM perspective, he has full access to both [[Amazon EC2|EC2]] and S3. It's important to note that the scope is limited to the bounds of the R&D account. Switching back to the Policy Generator, I paste his ARN in the principal box. After pasting in his ARN, I go to the Select Actions area. Scrolling down, I find ListBucket. After specifying that permission, I need to specify the ARN for the bucket in question. Switching back to the tab with the bucket information, I can go ahead and copy the bucket ARN right within the Edit Bucket Policy section. With that in my clipboard, I switch back to the Policy Generator and paste in the link for the ARN. With those steps completed, I click Add Statement. The next thing I want to do is allow Rajesh to get objects from within the bucket. While I could go back to my development account to retrieve Rajesh's ARN, I can see it right on the screen in the principal section, so I'm simply going to highlight it on this screen and paste it in to the principal box. Since I want Rajesh to be able to access the contents, the effect remains as Allow.
>
> **[6:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/creating-an-s3-bucket-policy?u=76281980&t=383)** From the Actions list, I scroll down, then select GetObject. Finally, I need to specify the ARN for the bucket. I could switch browser tabs, but I'm just going to grab it from the resource section of the first statement I added to the policy. Copying that, I paste it into the ARN At the end, I append a slash and a star. That means Rajesh will be able to retrieve anything within that bucket. With those steps complete, I click Add Statement. Taking a moment to verify that the two statements look good, I proceed by clicking the Generate Policy button. In the modal window that appears, I select the entire JSON statement and copy it to my clipboard. Then I return to the Bucket Policy tab for the bucket in question, and paste it into the policy area. If I wanted, I could replace the statement IDs with something more descriptive. However, I'm just going to leave the unique statement IDs generated by the Policy Generator in place. After pasting in the policy, I scroll to the bottom and click Save Changes. Immediately, I get the confirmation message that the bucket policy was edited successfully.
>
> **[8:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/creating-an-s3-bucket-policy?u=76281980&t=480)** Scrolling down, I can see that my bucket policy is in place.

First, let's have a quick checkpoint quiz. Do you remember the four elements associated with bucket policies? The first is the effect, either allowing or denying an action. The second is the principal populated by the ARN, whose permissions you want to adjust. Then there's the action, which can get as granular as you'd like in terms of allowing or denying permissions. Finally, there's the resource, which is the ARN to which you want to adjust access. To help me illustrate this, I've created a couple of named profiles on my laptop. First, I've created a profile for Ari, the developer with access to production. Let's say she needs to resolve a performance issue and has to edit the Engine X configuration file. However, he's in a hurry and simply edits his command history. Fortunately, the bucket policy is there to save him.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/illustrating-s3-bucket-policies-with-cli?u=76281980&t=189)** Since his permissions are limited to listing the bucket's contents and retrieving its objects, he gets an access denied message. In addition to restricting access by IAM user, that's only the start of what you can do with bucket policies. They can be used to restrict access based on IP address, grant read access to anonymous users, and ensure that MFA is required to interact with a bucket. If you have a diverse set of complicated access rules for your S3 buckets, you can get it done with a bucket policy.

#### [Understanding S3 access control lists](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-s3-access-control-lists?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-s3-access-control-lists?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] S3 Access Control Lists, or ACLs, are another tool that exists for controlling access to objects stored in S3. Let's understand what they are and see how they work and appreciate why they are dangerous. The first thing to understand about ACLs is that they can apply to every object you put into S3. With literally trillions of objects, in S3, that's potentially a lot of ACLs. Imagine the chaos if each object was managed with its own unique ACL. Maintenance would be an administrative nightmare. Another thing to understand is that with S3 being one of the oldest services in AWS, access control lists came into being long before IAM policies and bucket policies existed. Let's get into the console to gain an appreciation of why you need to be very careful if you decide to use ACLs. Here I am logged into the web console, looking at my S3 buckets. Let's take a look at my public-assets bucket. Clicking on the name of that bucket takes me to a page that lists all of the objects in this bucket. The first thing we'll do is check the permissions.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-s3-access-control-lists?u=76281980&t=96)** Clicking on the Permissions tab, I can see that from an access standpoint, there's an indicator that objects within this bucket can be public. That is, the block all public access is off. Scrolling down a bit more, we can see that there is no bucket policy in place. In the Object Ownership section, this is where we need to pay attention. Note that under Object Ownership, bucket owner is preferred. This means that by default, new objects placed in this bucket are owned by the owner of the bucket. In the ACL section, we can see that only the bucket owner has permissions on the bucket itself. What does that mean practically? Let's scroll up to the top and click on the Objects tab. As we can see, there are a number of objects within this bucket. I'll pick the object at the bottom and open the link for that object in a new tab. Switching to that tab, I see the details for this object itself. Note that it has its own object URL. That's of the wonderful defining features of S3, the ability to make individual objects accessible over the web. I'm going to go ahead and try and open that URL
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-s3-access-control-lists?u=76281980&t=191)** in a new tab. Switching to that tab, we can see that access to this object over the public internet is denied. That's because the object itself is private. Now let's go back to the details page for this specific object and click on the permission section. Here, we can see the access control list for this specific object. I'm going to go ahead and click the edit button. This lets me edit the ACL for this object. I'm going to click the checkbox that enables read permission for everyone. That is public access. Notice how I get a red warning triangle. Scrolling down a bit more, I get a very clear message. Since making this change allows global access, AWS wants to make sure that I understand, in crystal clear fashion, the impact of this change. It's essentially a double warning. Immediately, I get the message that this ACL has been modified. Now let's switch back to the browser tab where I had opened the URL for this object. I'm simply going to refresh the tab. As you can see, I get a dramatically different result. Instead of permission denied, I'm able to see the contents of this object.

#### [S3 public access best practices](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/s3-public-access-best-practices?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/s3-public-access-best-practices?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Now that we've explored a variety of ways to enable public access, let's talk about some best practices for public access in S3. First things first, if you have the flexibility to use multiple accounts for S3 storage, use one account for all of your public assets and another account for your private assets. That way it's clean and simple. There's no chance of accidentally enabling public access within your private account. Use that global switch at the account level and just turn it off. Access control lists are a legacy construct. Just get rid of them, turn them off. If you really want to enable public access, use a bucket policy. Let's take a quick peek at how to do that right now by jumping into the AWS Console. Navigating back to our bucket, I'm going to go ahead and scroll down to the Object Ownership section. The first thing I'm going to do is disable ACLs by clicking the Edit button. Then I'll select the ACL disabled radio button and click Save changes. Navigating back to our pink car,

Access for that object is denied even though the ACL is in place since we disabled ACLs for the bucket. Suppose that we want every asset in this bucket to be public for use on a website, let's solve that with a bucket policy. Scrolling to the bucket policy section, I'm going to go ahead and click Edit, then add a new statement. The service I want to use is S3, so I type that in and click S3. The action I want everyone to be able to do is get an object, so I type that into the filter box and click GetObject. Now I need to specify the principle. In this case, I want it to be everybody on the planet, so I simply put a star in quotes. The resource, of course, would be the bucket arn followed by a slash, followed by a star. That means every single object in this bucket will be public. Scrolling down, I click Save changes. With that in place, everything in this bucket will be public. Clicking back on our pink car tab,
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/s3-public-access-best-practices?u=76281980&t=192)** I go ahead and click refresh. #### [Exploring pre-signed URLs](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/exploring-pre-signed-urls?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/exploring-pre-signed-urls?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Presigned URLs are a unique way to grant temporary access to objects in S3. With a presigned URL, people who don't have an AWS account are able to access S3 objects. Since presigned URLs expire, using them is a great way to enable time-limited access to assets you store in S3. Let's explore how presigned URLs work. Suppose a company stores marketing collateral in S3. Enzo is a developer with an IAM user account. Alternatively, he could have access to AWS using Identity Center. As part of an upcoming marketing campaign, he is tasked with making some time-sensitive collateral images accessible to the world. Instead of using access control lists on the individual S3 objects, he decides to generate a presigned URL on a private object. Part of the collateral contains coupons, which will expire. Because of this requirement, Enzo chooses to use presigned URLs as they become invalid after a period of time. Using his security credentials, Enzo generates a presigned URL set to expire after the marketing campaign is over.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/exploring-pre-signed-urls?u=76281980&t=94)** Enzo can embed the presigned URLs in websites or marketing emails. The marketing campaign goes out and people go to the website or open the marketing emails. When they do, the collateral is retrieved from S3 using Enzo's permissions. This is, because Enzo is the one who generated the presigned URL in the first place. When the presigned URL expires, external access is revoked as the object is private. AWS supports the generation of presigned URLs in a variety of development languages. Conveniently, AWS makes a plugin for [[Microsoft]] Visual Studio that makes it possible to generate a presigned URL without having to explicitly write a lot of software. Let's go into Visual Studio to see how this is done. Here I am logged into a machine with Visual Studio open. In order to install the toolkit for Visual Studio, I go to Extensions, Manage Extensions. From there, I can search for AWS. The first one that comes up is the AWS Toolkit for Visual Studio. I've already installed it just to save a bit of time. Closing the Extension Manager window,
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/exploring-pre-signed-urls?u=76281980&t=188)** I can access the toolkit under Extensions, AWS Toolkit. I'm going to go ahead and go to the Getting Started page. The first thing we need to specify is what we want access to. In this case, we want access to the AWS Explorer. Next, we can specify what type of credentials we're going to use to authenticate to AWS. What we could do is use an Identity and Access Management user role. In this case, we'd need to specify the profile name, access key ID, and associated secret key. For this demonstration, I'm going to go ahead and illustrate what it's like to use an IAM Identity Center role. The first thing I'll do is specify a profile name. The next thing I need to do is specify the start URL for Identity Center. In this browser, I'm at the Identity Center dashboard in my research account. Here I can conveniently grab the AWS access portal URL. Copying the URL to my clipboard, I switch back to Visual Studio. From there, I go ahead and paste the URL into the Start URL box. > **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/exploring-pre-signed-urls?u=76281980&t=282)** I could change the default region and SSO region. Now let's open up the AWS Explorer, which is using Enzo's profile. Expanding S3, I see a number of buckets. The one I'm interested in is sbn-research-presigned. Clicking into that bucket, we see that it contains one file, fall.jpeg. Let's see what it looks like to open this file within the S3 browser. From my Identity Center screen, I go to the S3 console. From there, I go to Buckets, then my presigned bucket. Clicking into fall.jpeg, I see the object URL. Copying that to my clipboard, I'll open a new browser tab and paste that in, excellent. As expected, access to this is denied, because it's not publicly accessible.
>
> **[7:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/exploring-pre-signed-urls?u=76281980&t=469)** The URL for this object is indeed private. Now let's get back to Visual Studio and create that presigned URL. Right clicking on the object in question, I'm going to go down and click Create Pre-Signed URL. Pre-populated are the name of the S3 bucket as well as the name of the object itself. What I can do is specify the point at which this URL will expire. Let's say we were running a July 4th promotion and we wanted the expiry to be on the 7th of July. Navigating to July 7th, I simply select that, and in this case, I'll have it expire just before midnight. I proceed by clicking the Generate button. It generates a long URL that I can go ahead and click on and see if it works. Wonderful, we can see in the browser tab that's popped open that the contents of the file is being displayed. Examining the URL, note that there's the name of the bucket, the object name, and then a bunch of parameters. The first being an access key that corresponds to Enzo's ID. Then the expiry time, and then a very, very long security token. That's the generated magic that allows time-limited access.
>
> **[9:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/exploring-pre-signed-urls?u=76281980&t=566)** Of course, if we remove any of those parameters and try and access the object, we get the access denied message. That makes sense as this is an unauthenticated request. Looking in another browser tab, I have the URL for a presigned URL that's already expired. Here, we can see that even if people still have the presigned URL after it expires, they no longer have access to the object it references. I think you'll agree that presigned URLs can be a real ally if you have the use case that corresponds to delivering time-sensitive or time-limited access to objects within S3.

#### [Understanding S3 Access Grants](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-s3-access-grants?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-s3-access-grants?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] S3 Access Grants are a security mechanism that lets you grant internal and federated users access to data in S3. Many organizations store a variety of assets in S3. If you have a relatively small amount of data in S3, you can control access through a combination of S3 bucket policies and IAM policies. However, as your data volumes grow and the number of different constituents accessing your data grows, you can run into challenges. That's because IAM policies have a maximum size of 5 kilobytes. While S3 bucket policies are more generous, they are capped at 20 kilobytes. As your data volumes continue to grow, you might find yourself simply out of space to implement the appropriate security controls. Fortunately, S3 Access Grants exist to help you provision granular access to S3 at scale. S3 Access Grants let you simplify S3 permissions. You can grant read-only access, write-only access, or read/write access. You can leverage Identity Center
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-s3-access-grants?u=76281980&t=94)** to create groups of users using your external corporate directory. You also have the option of granting access to IAM principles. After creating an access grants instance, you need to configure locations. Once you have locations in place, you can start creating grants. For example, you can create a grant that allows employees in your corporate directory who validate expenses, read-only access to a location that contains expense receipt images. You can write a separate grant to enable the IAM principles of your system administrators read/write access to a location containing [[System Configuration]] files. Note that you can't view data using access grants in the web console. You'll need to create an application or use the command line interface. Diving a little deeper, S3 Access Grants instances are the greatest level of access grant abstraction. Think of an access grants instance as a box that contains locations and grants. You can only have one access grants instance per region in an AWS account.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-s3-access-grants?u=76281980&t=188)** For example, if you're working in ap-south-1 Mumbai, you can only have one access grants instance, which would govern access to the S3 assets in that region. After creating an instance, you can define locations. Locations are destinations in S3. Since you can have multiple locations, you may have one that refers to all S3 buckets and others that are scoped to a single bucket. You can limit what can be accessed within a location using grants. You can have up to 1,000 locations per access grants instance. With multiple grants per location, you can create read-only grants, read/write grants, and write-only grants. From a limit standpoint, you can have 100,000 grants per grants instance. When it comes time for an application to use an access grant, it gets temporary credentials, which can be valid from 15 minutes to 12 hours. As I think you'll agree, while S3 access grants can get complex, they are one approach to solving the management of S3 permissions at scale.

#### [Understanding S3 Access Points](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-s3-access-points?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-s3-access-points?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] S3 Access Points are an alternative to Access Grants for managing access to S3 at scale. Access points come in two different flavors. With a standard access point, you enable external traffic to an S3 bucket. If you have more than one bucket, you need to provision another access point. However, it is possible to have multiple access points each with an independent access policy granting access to the same bucket. Each access point has a policy size limitation of 20 kilobytes. You can have up to 10,000 access points in a given region. Note that this is a soft quota that can be increased with a support request. One unique thing about access points is that it's possible to attach an access point to a VPC. The VPC you specify is locked when a VPC access point is created. Basically when you set it up, you can't change the VPC after the fact. That ensures that any traffic flowing through the VPC access point originates from that VPC. One thing to consider is where you implement security at the bucket or access point level. Certainly you can configure bucket policies
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-s3-access-points?u=76281980&t=97)** to govern access to the objects in the bucket. However, you can imagine things can get confusing when policies also exist for access points. Recall that from a size limitation perspective, bucket policies are limited to 20 kilobytes. A clean way to avoid policy confusion is to write your bucket policies such that they delegate permissions to the access points. That way, you can use access points for what they're designed for, which is controlling access to S3. This is also where you can see the power of scale as you can have many access points for a given bucket. Another thing to keep in mind is that access points allow for object operations only. This makes sense as it's kind of in the name. Access points are for access, not creating and deleting buckets. If you have global operations and are using S3 Cross-Region Replication, you may want to consider a multi-region access point. Suppose you have web assets stored in Ohio with Cross-Region Replication setup for Oregon and Mumbai. With a multi-region access point, S3 requests will be automatically routed to the closest bucket, minimizing latency.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-s3-access-points?u=76281980&t=192)** In this example, requests from India are serviced by the bucket in Mumbai. Similarly, requests from New York route to Ohio, and requests from Washington state are served by the bucket in Oregon. There are some considerations to think through in terms of setup, but if you have global needs, this capability is a significant benefit. You can also use access points to transform S3 data in real time. For example, if you want to ensure sensitive customer data doesn't leak when requested by a specific application, an Object Lambda Access Point can help. Starting with an access point in front of a bucket, you can write a function that strips sensitive information about your customers from an object. Putting an Object Lambda Access Point in front of the Lambda function, you can then put an Object Lambda Access Point in front of the function. That way, any application you connect to that Object Lambda Access Point will only ever see data after it's been processed by the Lambda function. I'm sure you appreciate how the various access point flavors can help you tailor your access needs to whatever dilemma you're trying to solve. So what's the difference
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-s3-access-points?u=76281980&t=287)** between access grants and access points? Access grants are limited to read/write permissions, while access points allow for additional object permissions, like creating and deleting objects. Access grants don't inherently include network controls, while access points can be publicly available or locked to a VPC. Access grants are ideal for building applications that fine tune access to individuals and groups, while access points [[Microsoft Excel|excel]] in distributing permissions to individual endpoints for shared datasets.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>

#### [Understanding data protection schemes](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-data-protection-schemes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-data-protection-schemes?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] There are a variety of controls available to help improve the security and auditability of data you store in S3. One control to be aware of is bucket versioning. Suppose you have two employees, Miguel and Guadalupe, who both have access to the corporate-secrets bucket. As the name implies, it contains various files, including a particularly sensitive spreadsheet with details about upcoming merger and acquisition activity. Guadalupe updates the spreadsheet with some new information about the upcoming merger. Miguel also has an update, but it doesn't contain Guadalupe's update. Unfortunately, Guadalupe's changes are lost. You can prevent this situation by editing the bucket properties to enable bucket versioning. Now, when Guadalupe changes a file, it will be stored as a new version within S3. Similarly, if Miguel accidentally overwrites Guadalupe's file without her changes, that new version is stored as version three. In this situation, Miguel could retrieve version two, which has Guadalupe's changes, add his changes, then upload those changes to S3.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-data-protection-schemes?u=76281980&t=94)** That would result in yet another version of this file. Of course, you pay for each version that you store, which can add up over time. Therefore, you need to be judicious about deciding if bucket versioning makes sense for your situation. One thing you can do to mitigate the cost of maintaining multiple versions of an object is use a lifecycle rule. For example, suppose you had those four versions of the spreadsheet and want to balance the cost and value of maintaining history. Keeping track of every detailed change to the data that goes into making an acquisition decision loses its value over time and at some point you only care about the final document. You can reduce your costs by implementing a lifecycle rule. Lifecycle rules can do many things, including moving the current version or non-current versions to less expensive S3 storage classes and removing non-current versions. For example, you could define a rule to remove any non-current version after a time period that works for you. For the sake of an example, let's say that's 30 days. On the 31st day, all non-current versions are removed. If version four was the most current,
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-data-protection-schemes?u=76281980&t=190)** every other version would disappear forever. Of course, you need to ensure that the rules you define don't violate any compliance obligations you may have. Another useful management feature is the ability to prevent objects from being modified. Suppose Miguel was under a litigation hold. Since litigation or legal holds require preventing the alteration, destruction, or tampering of evidence, you need to freeze assets Miguel has access to at the time the hold is put in place. If Miguel attempted to remove an object from the corporate-secrets bucket, that would violate the legal hold. By enabling object lock, the destructive action would not succeed, keeping the data in the required state that complies with pending legal action. Be advised that once you turn on object lock for a bucket, it's a one-way switch. You can't disable it or suspend versioning for that bucket. Another way to improve auditability is with server access logging. In this case, both Miguel and Guadalupe have access to that sensitive spreadsheet in the corporate-secrets bucket. Although they both are trusted employees,
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-data-protection-schemes?u=76281980&t=284)** you may need a record of when this file gets accessed and by whom for audit and compliance reasons. Fortunately, this can be accomplished using server access logging. To configure server access logging, you first need to create an additional bucket to serve as the destination for the server access logs. In the new bucket, you need to enable access control lists then edit the permissions to allow the S3 log delivery group read and write access to this bucket and its objects. In the original bucket, you can then update the properties, enabling server access logging, and specifying the new destination bucket. With server access logging in place, anytime either Miguel or Guadalupe interact with the corporate-secrets bucket, that activity will be logged in the logging bucket. If a destructive action takes place, you'll know exactly who did it and what happened. Keep in mind that server access logs are noisy. They will capture any interaction with the bucket, regardless of if it comes from the console, command line interface, or API. Anything from browsing a bucket to opening an object to getting an object to putting an object,
>
> **[6:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-data-protection-schemes?u=76281980&t=380)** it's all registered in the server access logs. You may also want to protect assets in S3 by replicating them in another region. Fortunately, this is straightforward to configure. Suppose you have a bucket containing web assets in us-east-2. To guard against a regional failure, you could create another bucket in another region, say us-west-2. Both the source and destination buckets need versioning enabled. Once you have both buckets in place, you adjust the management properties of the original bucket, specifying the new bucket as the destination. With that configuration in place, any time an existing object changes or a new object gets added to the original bucket, it gets replicated to the destination bucket. Keep in mind that if you're replicating across regions, there is an associated transfer fee.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Warnings:** keep in mind (2), be aware (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (2)
> **Prerequisites:** configure (2)
> **Env Vars:** api (1)

#### [S3 security and compliance best practices](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/s3-security-and-compliance-best-practices?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/s3-security-and-compliance-best-practices?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] S3 is the multi-tool of object storage and can be configured to support many different use cases. Let's step back and review some best practices for security and compliance in S3. First, enabling versioning on your S3 buckets keeps multiple versions of an object. This can help you recover from accidental deletions or overrides. Of course, you do pay for every object stored, so keep that in mind. Recall that you can mitigate that with a lifecycle policy. Apply the principle of least privilege by granting only the permissions required by the situation. Use bucket policies to define bucket level permissions and IAM policies for users and roles. S3 encrypts data at [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|rest]] by default. You can use S3 managed keys, custom KMS keys, or customer-provided keys for encrypting your data. If you have a need, enable S3 server access logging to track requests for access to your bucket. You can then analyze the logs for potential security risks or unauthorized access attempts. Use AWS CloudTrail to log all API calls to S3 and automate reviewing the CloudTrail logs for unusual activity.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/s3-security-and-compliance-best-practices?u=76281980&t=95)** Use AWS config to track S3 bucket configuration changes and compliance and set up rules to ensure buckets comply with your security policies. Use lifecycle policies to automatically transition data to different storage classes or delete objects after a specified period. Appropriately written lifecycle policies help manage data retention and reduce storage costs. Consider enabling multifactor authentication for deleting objects. This adds an additional layer of security. Every organization has its own unique legal and compliance burden. Whatever your organization is bound by, periodically review and update bucket policies, IAM policies, and any access control lists if you use them to ensure they meet security and compliance objectives. If you can get away from ACLs, do it. Enable object lock to prevent objects from being deleted or overwritten for a specified retention period or permanently. You can use object lock to meet regulatory requirements for data immutability. Remember, once you enable object lock on a bucket, you can't turn it off and bucket versioning needs to be enabled. Disable public access at the account or bucket levels
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/s3-security-and-compliance-best-practices?u=76281980&t=191)** unless absolutely necessary. If you have both public and private assets, consider separating them into distinct AWS accounts. That way, you can flip the account level switch to block public access in one account while leaving it on in the other account. Using VPC endpoints to securely connect to S3 without going through the public internet reduces exposure to external threats. If you operate at massive scale, use access grants, access points, and multi-region access points to make managing access easier. Use object Lambda functions and object Lambda access points to enforce [[Data Governance]] rules and perform data transformations like scrubbing or redacting sensitive data. Consider enabling Amazon Macie to discover, classify, and protect sensitive data stored in S3. Remember that Macie charges based on the amount of data scanned, so take that into account when designing your buckets. You don't want a nasty surprise. Set up CloudWatch alarms to monitor S3 metrics such as bucket size, number of objects, and request rates, and get [[Real-Time]] insights to potential security incidents.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|Rest]] (1), [[Data Governance]] (1), [[Real-Time]] (1)
> **Env Vars:** aws (3), iam (2), kms (1), api (1), vpc (1)
> **CLI Commands:** aws (3), make (1)
> **Prerequisites:** set up (2)
> **Analogies:** such as (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### [Challenge: S3 replication challenge](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/challenge-s3-replication-challenge?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/challenge-s3-replication-challenge?u=76281980&t=0)** (bright music)
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/challenge-s3-replication-challenge?u=76281980&t=5)** - [Instructor] With an understanding of all the various things you can do in S3, it's time for an S3 challenge. The first step is to create an S3 bucket. In the KMS challenge, you created a pair of KMS keys. Create this bucket in a Region that corresponds to one of your custom keys and enable encryption on the bucket you're creating with that custom KMS key. Make sure that you enable bucket versioning. In the other Region where you created your other KMS key in the previous challenge, create another bucket. Make sure you're also enabling encryption using your KMS key in this Region and that you enable versioning. Once the two buckets are in place, create a replication rule on the first bucket to replicate its contents to the second bucket. This rule should synchronize all objects. Please create a new IAM role to perform this synchronization and attach the policy you wrote in the KMS challenge that specifies the ability to perform decryption and encryption activities using the KMS keys in both of these Regions. After you've set up the replication rule, upload an object into the first bucket. After you've set up the replication rule,
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/challenge-s3-replication-challenge?u=76281980&t=101)** upload an object into the first bucket and validate that the replication rule you've put in place creates a copy in the second Region. Good luck.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

> **CLI Commands:** make (2)
> **Prerequisites:** set up (2)

Based off of your previous challenge, implement a rule that deletes all non-current versions in the first bucket after one day. After creating the rule, upload another copy of your original object, then wait 24 hours and check to verify that you only have one version of that object in the source bucket and that it's the most recent version. Your replica bucket should have both versions. Good luck.

### 8. Security Audits in AWS

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Understanding AWS Security Hub](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-aws-security-hub?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-aws-security-hub?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] AWS provides many tools to help you understand the security and compliance of your AWS account. With so many tools to choose from, it's easy to feel overwhelmed. Fortunately, AWS provides a tool called Security Hub that helps you centralize alerting. Let's explore how Security Hub can help make sense of a complicated operating environment. First off, you should know that Security Hub can aggregate across multiple AWS accounts with a simple configuration setting. It's important to understand that Security Hub is confined to a single region. If you operate in multiple regions and want to use Security Hub, you'll need to enable it in every region where you're using AWS services. There are many security and compliance tools available within AWS, and all of them can generate alerts. As the name implies, Security Hub centralizes alerts. These alerts can come from a variety of tools, including IAM Access Analyzer, Firewall Manager, GuardDuty, Inspector, and Macie. If you want to make use of the security checks within Security Hub, you'll need to have AWS Config set up. If you use other tools for alerting, like CrowdStrike Falcon for endpoint management, FireEye Helix for security information and event management, or PagerDuty for security orchestration,
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-aws-security-hub?u=76281980&t=96)** automation and response, they can be configured to integrate with Security Hub. Like the services with which it integrates, Security Hub runs continuously. You'll need to determine where it fits in your enterprise tool ecosystem. Security Hub does all of these things by comparing your account to acknowledged industry standards and best practices. Some standards, like the Center for Internet Security AWS Foundations Benchmark, are available to any organization with an AWS account. Others, like the [[PCI DSS|Payment Card Industry Data Security Standard]], only make sense if you process electronic payments. Security Hub has two primary constructs for reporting information. The first is a finding. Put simply, a finding is a potential security issue. It doesn't necessarily mean there is an issue, merely that one may exist. The second is what AWS calls an insight. An insight is a collection of findings that are related to each other. While there's a 30-day free trial for Security Hub, it's ultimately a service you'll have to pay for. It's up to you and your organization to decide whether or not you can realize significant time savings using Security Hub.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[PCI DSS|Payment card industry data security standard]] (1)

> **Prerequisites:** you'll need (3), set up (1)

#### [Rotating access keys](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/rotating-access-keys?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/rotating-access-keys?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Rotating Access Keys is one of those periodic maintenance tasks that you need to be on top of. Not only is key rotation a best practice, it is almost certainly something to be examined during a security audit. The following process is a safe method for rotating access keys. Starting with an understanding of where access keys are being used. The first step is to create a new access key and its associated secret. This can be done via the web console, command line interface, or a vi. You'll have to update any existing configuration file that uses an access key by replacing the secret and key with the ones you just created. The credential report within Access Analyzer can help you identify credentials and use and the user's section of the IAM web console will give you a visual on access key age. After updating your existing configurations, you need to perform a regression test using the newly created access key. #### [Understand AWS Artifact](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understand-aws-artifact?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understand-aws-artifact?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Every organization has compliance obligations to meet. AWS created a service called Artifact to facilitate access to documentation as to how AWS is doing its part when it comes to compliance. Recall that in terms of responsibility, AWS is accountable for securing the cloud itself. That includes all of the infrastructure that comprises [[Amazon Web Services (AWS)|Amazon Web Services]]. Meanwhile, you have the responsibility for securing how you use the tools AWS makes available. So as an example, if you misconfigure a security group, you're accountable for what happens as a result. Your role is to make sure that you are using the tools AWS provides to ensure the integrity and safety of the data with which you're entrusted. In order to access Artifact, you need to have administrative access to your AWS account. Of course, this access can be delegated using permissions within IAM. Once you have access, you're able to view and download documents related to how AWS maintains compliance. Of course, that is all in the context of the shared responsibility model. It is imperative
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understand-aws-artifact?u=76281980&t=92)** that you have clarity on your compliance obligations and how they are impacted by your use of AWS. You can also accept agreements within Artifact. For example, if you operate in Australia, you need to comply with the Australian Notifiable Data Breach Addendum. In the United States, if you deal with personal health information in AWS, you will need a Business Associate Addendum. You can review this non-disclosure agreement protected documentation and accept the associated agreements using Artifact. If you are using organizations to manage multiple AWS accounts, you can choose to accept agreements at the account or organization level. With an understanding of what Artifact is, let's get into the console and see how it can help you prove compliance. From the main search bar, I'll simply search for Artifact. Clicking the resulting link takes me to the Artifact homepage. I have a couple of options. The first is to view reports, and the second is to view agreements. Let's explore the reports first. Clicking the reports link takes me to the reports page. As of this recording,

> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understand-aws-artifact?u=76281980&t=185)** there are 279 different AWS reports available. Selecting that report, I have the option to download it. Clicking the download link brings up a modal window. Note that the terms and conditions state that I will not share this report with anyone as it is considered sensitive data that is owned by AWS. However, displaying the contents of this report in this video would be a violation of the terms, so I'm not going to do that now. Let's take a peek at the Agreements section. I'll do so by clicking on Agreements in the left-hand navigation. Here we can see the Australian Notifiable Data Breach Addendum
>

Now, the AWS Business Associate Addendum is active for this account. Similarly, if I no longer wish to process

#### [Understanding Trusted Advisor](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-trusted-advisor?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-trusted-advisor?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] AWS has built a tool called Trusted Advisor into the web console that helps you operate your AWS account as efficiently as possible. Let's explore what Trusted Advisor can do for you. Trusted Advisor is available for free in every AWS account. With business level paid support, Trusted Advisor offers comprehensive best practices checks against your account. Without a paid support plan, the checks are less complete. Currently, Trusted Advisor offers insights into six major categories, cost optimization, performance, security, fault tolerance, service limits, and [[Operational Excellence]]. Let's explore the cost optimization portion of Trusted Advisor. At the free tier, mo cost optimization checks are included. With a paid support plan, the cost optimization section looks for AWS Compute and network resources in your account that aren't being heavily used or reserved capacity you've purchased that is idle. Trusted Advisor also keeps track of storage generations. So for example, if you are using gp2 EBS volumes
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-trusted-advisor?u=76281980&t=96)** instead of gp3, that would be flagged. The second Trusted Advisor category is performance. This category evaluates your AWS account for things that could impact the delivery of your services. At the free tier, no performance related checks are included. With a paid plan, one of the big advantages is being able to talk with an actual human about performance concerns. Similar to other Trusted Advisor categories, performance is examined across multiple AWS services. Some recommendations, such as high CPU on [[Amazon EC2|EC2]] instances, can cause you to consider alternate configurations. For example, suppose Trusted Advisor reported consistently high CPU on an EC2 instance. If load on the machine were to spike, the CPU could become saturated and your customers would be disserviced. To address this possibility, expending the effort to configure an auto-scaling group to dynamically [[React.js|react]] to high CPU load might be worth the effort. Security is another category available within Trusted Advisor. AWS takes security very seriously. As such, the free tier includes more checks
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-trusted-advisor?u=76281980&t=190)** than any other Trusted Advisor category. The free tier checks that the root credentials for your account have been protected with multifactor authentication. Trusted Advisor also checks your account to ensure IAM users have been configured. Using an IAM user as opposed to the root account credentials is a best practice, which is why this check exists. It also checks to see if you have assets which are publicly accessible, including EBS and RDS snapshots, as well as S3 buckets. An additional check in the free tier has to do with [[Network Security]]. Trusted Advisor will give you a report on any network security groups in your account that allow unrestricted access to common administrative ports. For example, ports 1433 and 1434 are well known as the ports for administrative access to the [[Microsoft SQL Server]] database, and 22 is universally known as the port for SSH. Trusted Advisor will let you know if your network is configured to allow unrestricted access to these ports. As you would expect, the paid plan offers more comprehensive checks across more AWS services. Checks within the paid tier include how CloudTrail
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-trusted-advisor?u=76281980&t=286)** and load balancer security is configured, if you are rotating IAM access keys, and more. Another category is fault tolerance. As with many other Trusted Advisor categories, the free tier includes no fault tolerance checks. However, with a paid plan, the fault tolerance section looks at how AWS resources in your account are configured for [[Resiliency]]. An example of a fault tolerance check is how load balancers are configured in your account. If a load balancer exists but has no EC2 instances associated with it, the fault tolerance section of Trusted Advisor would flag that for your review. Trusted Advisor also helps you keep track of the service limits in your account at no additional cost. As the name implies, Trusted Advisor looks at how you are using AWS services and if you are approaching the service limit that is configured for your account. For example, suppose a professor teaching a network and security class wants to provide logical network isolation for each of her 18 students. To do that, she decides to provision each student a virtual private cloud, or VPC. However, five is the default service limit
>
> **[6:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-trusted-advisor?u=76281980&t=383)** for the number of VPCs in an account. Trusted Advisor will alert the professor once she has consumed 80% of the service limit. In this case, when she configures her fourth VPC, she will be using 80% of the available five. Most service limits are soft limits in AWS and can be augmented by opening a support case, asking for a limit adjustment, and providing justification. Trusted Advisor can provide recommendations related to the operational effectiveness of your account. At the free tier, no operational excellence related checks are included. With a paid plan, Trusted Advisor includes a variety of checks that are hallmarks of operational excellence. Investigating and remediating these recommendations can help prepare you for your next audit. For example, Trusted Advisor checks for configurations related to maintaining audit logs, such as a load balancer not having access logging turned on, or a CloudTrail trail not sending logs to CloudWatch. Trusted Advisor also has version related checks. For example, Trusted Advisor will flag any RDS database you're using if it isn't configured to automatically apply minor version upgrades.
>
> **[7:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-trusted-advisor?u=76281980&t=479)** Trusted Advisor has a variety of automation related checks. For example, Trusted Advisor can report on EC2 instances not managed by systems manager. Trusted Advisor also includes checks that make it hard for people to make regrettable mistakes. For example, Trusted Advisor will check your RDS database clusters and elastic load balancers for deletion protection.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

> **Env Vars:** aws (11), cpu (4), ec2 (4), iam (3), rds (3)
> **CLI Commands:** aws (11), make (2), ssh (1)

#### [Understanding Audit Manager](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-audit-manager?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-audit-manager?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Audits are an inevitable part of [[Business Operations]]. AWS Audit Manager helps you position yourself for a successful audit by continuously watching over how you use AWS and how that fits with your compliance obligations. To help put Audit Manager in context, it helps to focus on some basic terminology. A control is a compliance requirement. For example, if you process payment cards, a control is the need to protect cardholder data. Controls that are tailored to meet a specific compliance requirement are known as custom controls. If you have controls that can satisfy multiple compliance obligations, they're known as common controls. A collection of controls, custom controls and common controls that when taken together satisfy an audit objective is known as a framework. AWS provides a number of frameworks tailored to a variety of objectives from AWS best practices to [[PCI DSS|PCI-DSS]] and the European Union's General Data Protection Regulation. An assessment is using a framework in your environment. Assessments run automatically in the background, gathering data about how your environment conforms to the controls in your specified framework.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-audit-manager?u=76281980&t=96)** Audit Manager gathers this data by running resource assessments against your AWS configurations. Resource assessments execute at the AWS service level, such as VPC, S3, [[Amazon EC2|EC2]], et cetera. Resource assessments collect evidence that illustrates specifically how your AWS configuration complies with your chosen framework. At the end of the day, Audit Manager generates an assessment report. An assessment report aggregates the evidence related to a framework organized by the controls in that framework, so you can easily prepare documentation for your auditors. Imagine your business running in AWS. Due to a misconfiguration, a security group on an EC2 instance has left an administrative port open to the internet. While this certainly poses a [[Security Risk]], it also represents a potential compliance issue. With Audit Manager running, you could configure an assessment. In turn, the assessment would conduct a number of resource assessments, gathering data about how you are using AWS. In turn, any evidence gathered about the controls in the assessment are stored in the S3 bucket you specified when configuring Audit Manager.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/understanding-audit-manager?u=76281980&t=194)** Available for further review, an internal audit manager, or someone who works as his or her delegate, reviews all of the findings and associated evidence. Since assessments span multiple services, there are likely to be an internal team of delegates evaluating the findings. In this situation, you would need to remediate the security group identified in the assessment. The entire purpose of Audit Manager is to reduce the amount of effort you have to expend preparing for an audit.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Amazon EC2|Ec2]] (2), [[Business Operations]] (1), [[PCI DSS|Pci-dss]] (1), [[Security Risk]] (1)
> **Env Vars:** aws (9), ec2 (2), pci (1), dss (1), vpc (1)
> **CLI Commands:** aws (9)
> **Definitions:** known as (2), is a  (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (1), such as (1), imagine (1)
> **Prerequisites:** configure (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### [Prepare for a security audit](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/prepare-for-a-security-audit?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/prepare-for-a-security-audit?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] You need to be ready for the inevitable audit. Be prepared to demonstrate how your AWS configuration meets your various compliance obligations. First, you must understand what the audit is asking you to verify. Is it a financial, security, or a compliance audit? You need to identify the applicable standards for your situation, anything from [[PCI DSS]] for payment card transactions to GDPR For European operations. Clearly define the scope of your audit, including which services, resources, data, and configurations are included. With the scope understood, identify the security and compliance resources available to you within AWS. This includes AWS's compliance programs and related documentation available in Artifact. Don't forget to include current security best practices documentation to ensure that you're up-to-date with the latest recommendations. In addition, review your existing governance and access controls. Specifically, ensure that IAM policies are configured correctly and abide by the principle of least privilege. Also confirm that multifactor authentication is enabled for all IAM users and your root accounts.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/prepare-for-a-security-audit?u=76281980&t=96)** If you're using more than one AWS account, ensure you have your AWS organization set up to centralize account management and billing. Make sure to review your logging and monitoring configuration. Specifically, ensure that you have Cloud Trail enabled to log all API calls and account activity, as well as Cloud Watch for monitoring and alerting on metrics and logs. Consider enabling VPC Flow Logs to capture information about traffic between the internet and your AWS environment. You also need to verify your data protection and encryption approaches. Specifically, your data in transit and at [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|rest]] should be encrypted, either with native AWS encryption or with custom KMS keys. Review your S3 bucket policies, access grants, and access points to restrict access and ensure data protection. Make sure you've developed and tested your approach to backing up data and keeping it safe. Remember, AWS backup and Elastic Disaster Recovery can be of real assistance in protecting your data. Internally, conduct risk assessments and compliance checks. Recall that Config can assess your AWS configurations and that Security Hub centralizes security alerting
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/prepare-for-a-security-audit?u=76281980&t=193)** and compliance status. Consider running penetration tests on your environment to get a greater sense of confidence. After you conduct all of these internal reviews, you'll need to prepare documentation for both internal and external auditors. Audit Manager can help you generate your documentation as it includes frameworks and associated controls. Of course, Cloud Trail and S3 server access logs can help document actions. Don't forget, you can also access AWS documentation using Artifact. In the spirit of continuous organizational awareness, ensure that your people understand compliance requirements and security best practices, and that you've taken a role-based instead of person-based approach to access management. To best position yourself for success, it's a great idea to use the Center for Internet Security Benchmark for securing AWS. Recall that Security Hub greatly simplifies comparing your environment to this benchmark. If you use an external authentication store like [[Active Directory]], you must do all user verifications upstream of AWS. Regardless, if you use tools like GuardDuty and Detective,
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/prepare-for-a-security-audit?u=76281980&t=288)** recall that Cloud Trail provides a wonderful audit trail of activity within your AWS account. If you don't use a native AWS service to assist you, strongly consider using a third-party tool that can scan for irregular access patterns in your Cloud Trail logs.

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/next-steps?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Thank you for watching the complete guide to [[AWS Security]] and compliance. It's been a real pleasure accompanying you on this exploration of security and compliance concepts and tools. I hope you gained an appreciation for what a robust and complicated platform AWS represents, and that you have a firm grasp on how to architect a secure, compliant environment. Keep in mind that AWS is relentlessly innovative and continues to improve the tools available to help you secure your operations and facilitate compliance. I hope you've had fun and learned a lot. At this point, you should have a good understanding of the AWS shared responsibility model. You should feel comfortable designing, testing, managing, and debugging access with IAM in a single account, across multiple accounts, and with federated identities. You are well-versed in the detection and response tools AWS offers, like Config, GuardDuty, Security Lake, and more. You know how to protect your networks and applications with the myriad firewall offerings AWS provides. With data as your most critical electronic asset, you know how to protect it with the Key Management Service and how to protect your secrets with Secrets Manager.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-aws-security-and-compliance-management/next-steps?u=76281980&t=96)** You understand the nuanced security controls that are unique to S3. You're also prepared to think through the tools that can help you with your audit obligations. I urge you to never stop learning. For starters, check out the other courses in the library. The AWS Security Blog is worth adding to the list of things you can continue to read. AWS does a great job of maintaining accurate documentation for its services. I highly recommend looking it over, especially as it continues to evolve. AWS user groups are a great online resource, as are local meetups. Remember, it's always the people. I've also found Stack Overflow to be a wonderful resource for checking and discussing issues, as is AWS's re:Post platform. If you're interested more broadly in security, the Center for Internet Security is a great resource. Above all, I encourage you to learn by doing. Remember, few complete guides are truly complete in a dynamically changing environment, and that there's always more to learn. Thanks again, and good luck.
